RATING: 6
Phew! Where to start. A grand scale game that plays quickly compared to others of this nature. The many mechanisms fit so well together. The game appears to scale very well between 2 & 7 players. Mutliple playings should be rewarded by new and interesting situations and approaches. However, I am becoming increasingly tired of some of the fiddly rules which don't really add anything to the game and it would have been nice to see those developed out. Rating is dropping (originally an 8). The alliance mechanism is still excellent however.

RATING: 9.5
Sublime!  Sophisticated, deep, multi-layered and brain-burning. Love it!

The Alliance structure makes it close all the way through and there is enough variation in the tiles to keep every game fresh.

It is interesting to compare my feelings about this game and it's brother in depth - AoS.  I can admire and appreciate AoS, but love SoE.  For me, AoS is a clever game for accountants, SoE a clever game for adventurers and fighters.

SoE plays well from 4 up, but really it's with 5 and more that it shines - and odd numbers work best of all.  Don't expect a short game though -  this one lasts between 3 and 4 hours.

RATING: 9
Rating of 9 based on one play, but this game earns every point. The bidding for shifting alliances is pure genius. Typical Wallace management of negative attributes, which I love.

UPDATE: After some thought following a second play, I'm lowering my rating a point to 8. I love it, but the game is just soul-crushing, and because of that I can't see wanting to play too often. Still, amazing design. The rules for crossing the rope are inspired.

UPDATE2 (Dec '13): Back to 9 after play No. 3. I did terribly, but had a great time playing. It's a clever game.

RATING: 8.5
A very well-done game. Some horribly fiddly movement rules aside, it's easy to understand and there's quite a lot of action. Because armies can move around the board so quickly, I always feel as though I'm walking a tightrope halfway through. Refreshingly different feeling than El Grande (to which it is oddly similar), which certainly isn't static but feels more constrained.
Unsure if the odd dice rules are a problem or not - as others have noted, there are typically one or two critical rolls throughout the game and luck can play a major role. So to speak.

RATING: 3.7
:cry::snore::snore::snore::snore::snore::snore: This rating is really more about my nearly non-existent desire to ever play this again (another bummer, as I was fairly interested in trying it). I mean, it's probably a good game, but a lot of times I don't really "get" Martin Wallace games or his style. It felt more like work than play, went on way too long for what it was (almost an hour of rules, plus 90 minutes for one war with six people), and just had too many things going on in too many directions for me to enjoy it. You probably need to already know the rules and tiles to enjoy it, and I don't think it's particularly worth the investment. We scored the endgame after one war. I think I was third or fourth, despite being the only one to lose a ship and getting clocked out of South America. The exception is that I might be convinced to like it as 2P, as then it would be a regular 1v1 wargame, which I grok better than this kind of wild, non-linear free-for-all. However, I also suspect that approximately nobody who actually owns this would WANT to play it as a 2P, because presumably it's intended to be in the multiplayer wargame genre, and there are already better 2P wargames anyway.

RATING: 6
There are some Euro games, where you feel that the theme is just tacked on, and it doesnt feel immersive.  In others, the theme and mecahnics match perfectly, and you really feel engrossed in the play of the game.

Then there is Struggle of Empires, where the game is as realistic as possible in the execution of its Slavery mechanic.  It really makes you feel like you aredeveloping navies off the coast of africa, shipping slaves off to plantations in north america.  Never let it be said that this game lacks in theme!  It does a great job of making you feel like an absolute piece of shit, destroying the lives of all these lesser peons, all those poor folks in the world who had the misfortune to be born with less privilege.

RATING: 9.5
Great, great game. Plenty of tough decisions and you always wish you could have one more action. Incredible theme sucks you into the era with shifting alliances and the constant tension of trying to expand while keeping the economic/population/unrest forces at bay. Just enough luck with dice rolls and placement of country markers at the start of each war to make things interesting, but not enough to offset the skill and planning of the players. Tons of replayability with the large number of tiles, and random country markers to convert to control.

RATING: 8
Hitherto, I've either found fault with Martin Wallace's games or they have not been to my taste.  In my view Struggle is pretty close to faultless.  As ever, there are lots of clever mechanics; the alliance auction, improvement and ally tiles, the threat of unrest, the need to balance areas earning income and victory points.  In Struggle they all mesh together really well.  I gripe about downtime in turn-based games with many options.  It feels less of an issue with Struggle because you will want to keep a close eye on your opponents' actions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First play was 3p, which is strongly influenced by the 2v1 alliance that happens every turn, and the necessity to ensure everyone is the "1" for one of the three wars. Interesting system, would certainly play again, interested to try 4 or 5. The key with battles seems to be to only fight when the odds are heavily on your side-- don't go in and hope to roll well, as the costs to engage are high.

RATING: 6
This is a decent dice throwing war game.  You could do worse than this if you wanted to play a long multi-player "conquest of the world" war game.  

However, it incorporates two VERY annoying mechanics that should have been edited out of the game.  First, every time you roll a seven with your two combat dice, you lose a unit.  What?  Why?  Because, life, and war, are unpredictable. Great, every time I roll a seven, how about I get something good in the game, because life is unpredictable?  Is that a good mechanic? Hint:  edit out this rule.

Second, every time you transport troops, or move ships to half the board, you roll one dice to see if you lose the ship, or lose the movement.  Because....life can be random.  It might seem thematic, but it ends up feeling dumb if it happens to you, and not others.

The "use the difference between your combat dice" is novel in this game, but for me, added an extra level of annoyance to the act of dice rolling.  Ready, set, try to roll two dice, with a maximum spread between them, and do not add up to seven!?  Wow, how does one even "wish" for that to happen in your mind?  How about trying to roll a straight with 3 dice? Or three dice, with a difference of 1 and 2 between them!  Fun?  

Sure, the idea here is to roll two dice to dependably create low numbers. Exciting?  Nope.  Why not just use a digital random number generator which generates numbers between 0 to 5, with greater weight being given to the lower numbers?  This would feel flat to me, just as the dice rolling in this game fell very flat to me.

Admittedly, the auctioning of alliances likely feels clever and interesting to hard-core wargamers, but as best I could tell, this mechanic has absolutely no real thematic connection to the world of this game, or reality.       

In the final analysis this is a game that feels like it was designed for wargamers who have tired of their titles, and want to play a variant on the same old-same old war game.  The game thus feels like a very well dressed version of Risk, without the fun.  And did I mention the game is too long with lots of boring down time?

RATING: 8
{1 play, 5 player} Wow.  You have to know that a) I'm not that big a fan of Martin Wallace games, b) I'm not really into the whole area influence, psudeo war-gamey thing, and c) I blanche at most games that take 3+ hours to play.  Three stikes... and yet I was knocked out!  I hope my opinion does not drop with future play (assuming I can get in some future play), because this one just all gelled together for me.  Love the alliances, didn't mind the dice rolling, and was absorbed enough to not feel the length.  A real surprise winner for now.

*** TWO YEARS LATER ***

Ha!  Well, I've yet to get in that second game.  And I may never, though I'm not sure why.  I think having the time to really let my feelings for the game gel and mature made me realize that, though I liked it, I don't think I'll like it as much the next time, especially for 3+ hours.  And so, in this case, I think I've avoided the game specifically so that I can remember my one and only play fondly, as a game I truly enjoyed.  I'm leaving my 8 rating, though, as I think it deserves it.

RATING: 7.5
Aunque en el fondo es un Eurogame de mayorías, la presencia de dado le hace ser un poco menos predecible y un poco más divertido de lo habitual.

La fase de subasta de alianzas es realmente interesante y las losetas especiales le dan variación y una pequeña evolución que lo hace parecer un juego de civilizaciones (aunque no lo sea). Tampoco es que vaya muy sobrado de "chrome" pero al menos esos detalles le dan un mínimo sabor.

Por contra el juego es largo y con menos de 5 jugadores no creo que funcione demasiado bien. También se le puede achacar que si te echan muy pronto de la partida es muy complicado (o directamente imposible) meterte de nuevo en la lucha por la victoria.

He de reconocer que pese a que las mayorías las detesto, el juego está bien pensado y me parece de lo mejor de Wallace, junto con Brass.

RATING: 8
As "everyone" notes, fantastic alliance/bidding system. Decent enough core game. RATING: 6
This is very long game - don't be fooled.  The game play is interesting though, but it depends on the players for balance since the game has no built in balancing mechanic.  It is easy to go after the colonies for the points, but a solid presence in Europe is essential to scoring points.  You likely won't win if you ignore the colonies, but you'll do much better than you might expect.  At its heart this is a negotiation game.  It is easy to think that the opponents during a war are in fact your enemies, but like in the times the game models, alliances are for convenience and do not reflect the real power or threat.  Picking your 'opponent' to be on your side in a war can be a critical move but one easy to overlook if you are vieing for revenge over a lost territory. [GTI: AGSD]

RATING: 9
This is definitely a niche game for a specific type of player.  At 3  hours and many may find this game too long or may not care for the direct conflict.  Love the bidding for alliances which is key to success.  Make sure that you don't end up outnumbered 1 against 2 on the european continent.   If you allow this to happen it's not going to be pretty.  The more players you have the more important naval forces are.

Plays best with more players.  A 7 player game with new players can take 4+ hours.  I think that an odd number of players is better as it makes everyone more uncomfortable and the bidding process more important.  The bidding is the best and most important aspect of the game.  This is a game where Kingmaking is build into the mechanics.  Always position yourself as the underdog, otherwise you will be lined up for execution in the bidding.

I bid high in comparison to others, but on the other hand the game unfolds more to my liking.

RATING: 7
Long game, even with three players.  I believe it can be quite unbalanced with three players, especially if the others outbid with their advantage and repeat the same 2-on-1 alliance.

Everybody seems to be angry for the whole game.  I believe the randomness in critical situations really frustrates all the players.

The wide assortment of free-action tiles makes for a lot of interesting combinations.  It's a little like Puerto Rico in that manner, except you can just buy Victory Points with gold.

Although Army Training and War Machine were quite useful, stacking up multiple Reserves seemed to be a real winner.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Muy buen juego. No es de los míos pero lo cortés no quita lo valiente, y este juego de valentía va "sobrao".
Cada jugador representa una de las naciones poderosas del XVIII, y lucha en el tablero por conseguir, con el sistéma de mayorías, y en tres recuentos distintos a lo largo de la partida, la mayor puntuación.
Lo que hace de él especial, a mí modo de ver, son cuatro cosas:
- A través de una puja, con pelas, se consiguen fijar el orden del turno (importantísimo, no siempre será bueno jugar primero y no siempre malo jugar el último) y también se forman la alianzas (más que importante, vital). De tal manera que los jugadores primero, tercero,  quinto y séptimo formarán una alianza y los jugadores segundo, cuarto y sexto formarán la otra alianza de la mesa. Los jugadores de una misma alianza no se pueden atacar entre sí. Estas dos cosas hacen que la fase de puja sea una delicia. 
- El sistéma de puntuación. A lo largo de la partida, y en tres ocasiones distintas, se contará quien tiene mayoría de contadores políticos en cada región que puntúa en el tablero. Pero lo que lo hace distinto es que este recuento ocurre tres veces a lo largo de la partida. Y acto seguido, después de cada recuento de puntos, se hace una nueva puja de alianzas... Estas dos cosas van ligadas y hace que ocurra el famoso dicho de " al que destaca, placa, pacla".
- Las acciones del jugador son pocas, sencillas, intuitivas y fáciles de ejecutar. Esto hace que seis/siete tíos jueguen un muy buen juego en poco más de 3 horas. Un gran mérito. 
- El tema del Unrest es interesante. En cualquier momento te puedes hipotecar para hacer efectivo un pago (salvo en el momento de la puja) a razón de un unrest por cada dos monedas (un añito después Wallace usará un sistéma parecido en el Railroad Tycoon). Pero el jugador que llegue a 20 o más al finalizar la partida es descalificado y no puntúa, amén de que los dos jugadores que más unrest consigan al finalizar la partida tendrán una penalización en puntos brutal (creo que 7 y 4 puntos respectivamente). Otra manera de conseguir unrest sería por cada tropa (tile de cartón) que te peten en una batalla. 
Por cierto. tres cosas básicas para poder jugar bien: Cuidadín con las alanzas, el dinero y sobre todo la presencia en Europa. Las colonias decantan la victoria a favor de los jugadores que mejor lo hayan hecho en Europa. 

Lo malo, a mi modo de ver, es la temática y los componentes (bendito plástico de FFG!)... Me imagíno un TI3 basado en este juego y creo que sería una compra segura... Lo que refuerza mi teoría de que a mí me entran los juegos por los ojos.... 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Kb. 2 évet vártam rá, hogy kipróbálhassam, bár nem az ideális létszámmal, mert csak 4 fő jött össze, de így is megérte szerintem!
A játéknak vannak pozitívumai, de néhány negatívum sajnos rányomja a bélyegét.
Először is a licit zseniális, már 4-en se volt rossz, de 7-en egész érdekes lehet. Sehol se láttam még ilyet, hogy a szöveségeseket licittel határozzuk meg. A szabály maga nem hosszú, de a sokféle lapka miatt nem annyira könnyű (plusz lásd a néhány szokásos Wallace féle apró kivételt, pl. Törökország esetében. meg hogy ki kinek a szomszédja, stb.). A lapkák változatossá teszik a játékot, van pár egész ötletes köztük. A végkifejlet nagyon izgalmasan alakult nálunk, végig néhány ponton belül volt mindenki, nem lehetett tudni, kit kellene ütni, ki a rivális. A kivitel szép, bár elég puritán (a játék előtt valaki lefitymálta a design-t, de szerintem egy 2004-es játéktól ez bőven elég...nem a külsőségek miatt népszerű gondolom.) A játékvégi büntetés nagyon ötletes módon kompenzál a játék közben elköltött pénzekért (szerintem ötletesebb, mint bármely másik általam ismerte Wallace féle hitel- mechanizmus).
Negatívumok: frusztráló a kockadobás szerepe. A csatában akár 5 különbség is adódhat kockadobásból, ezt nagyon nehéz kompenzálni, simán leverhető egy jóval erősebb sereg is. Ráadásul ott az a fránya 7-es dobás, ami annak ellenére, hogy (ha jól számolom) csak 1/6 eséllyel fordul elő, nálunk elég sokszor előfordult, és az a két ember lett az utolsó, aki legtöbbször dobott 7-est... A hajózásnál is van szerencsefaktor, de az nem bosszant, ott eltalálták a jó arányt. És még a háborúk elején a zsákból húzott 10 lapka is elég nagy szerencsetényező, bár a lapkák eloszlása ismert a játék elején...
Dícsértem a játékvégi minuszpontokat a végén. Nos az alapkoncepció jó, de a pontozás maga igazságtalan. Hogy egy példával érzékeltessem: ha a 4 játékosnak a játék végén 19,19,19 ill. 0 lázadás pontja van, akkor a kapott minusz pontok rendre: 7,7,7 ill 4 !!! No comment. Egyébként különlegesség, hogy kvázi nullázódhat a pont, ez viszont nem kiesést jelent, mert csak a játék végén derül ki, inkább ötletes pénzköltés-visszatartó erőnek nevezném, tehát ez nálam a pozitív kalapban van.
Összességében jó, de az ajánlott nagy létszám, és a bő 1 óra szabály + bő 3 óra játékidő (7-en gondolom 4-5 óra) miatt nem kerülhet elő sokat...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The great thing about this game is the bidding for forced alliances.  Basically, players are forced to be in one of two groups.  If you are in the same group as another player, you cannot attack them... which is Awesome!  I can only imagine alliances being even more awesome with an odd number of players.

Struggle of Empires has a lot of randomness that can really hose you, but I made peace with it early.  Keep in mind that this is coming from someone who usually dislikes randomness.  An example of some pretty random stuff is the dice based combat.  Instead of adding up the total of two dice (like in most games,) your attack value is instead the difference of your own two dice! To make combat even more random, any player who rolls a seven also loses 1 unit.  This can result in some pretty random shifts in power, but for some strange reason, I thinks it's brilliant. 

Unfortunately there are a few artistic decisions that could have been made better, but I guess that can be said of most Warfrog games.  To be honest, I'd probably rate SoE even higher if the game didn't require "fan" made player aids, if the tiles were larger, and if more wood was used instead of cardboard chits.

RATING: 10
Its long, it can be brutal, but it is satisfying. The auctions for alliances is the best way I've seen to handle multiplayer diplomacy games, since it limits how many players can pounce on the leader. Everyone has a say in how these alliances are set, including the leaders, and allies don't have to cooperate. There are even ways for allies to indirectly attack each other. All of these in a highly thematic package.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I set-up the game board, read the rules over 20+/- times (and still missed critical areas which just seem to sneak right between the eyes) and gave it a go.  I appreciate how neatly and easily the counters and tiles separate, a sign of good quality (and having punced many a wargame sheet, I know good, bad and ugggggly).  I think I've played it incorrectly several times, but I am learning--I hope.
 
Well, it is not a wargame by any stretch of the imagination, but as a strategy game of area control, it appears to be quite good.  Combat is so abstracted that it makes the battle card system in Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage look absolutely sophisticated (and that's not good).  When does the desire for simplicity and ease of play become an "issue"?  Would it have increased the complexity too much to have given particular nations certain combat benefits that match their historical strengths.  For example, the British fleet was the best in the world, and it should have a +1 advantage in any fight, end of story.  Perhaps the Prussian army, when fighting in the German States, should have a +1 advantage.  Food for thought.  

The mechanism(s) for making strategy decisions are quite excellent, and add a high degree of enjoyable tension.  The greater the number of players, the more likely that you will not acquire every improvement tile that you might want

I do find it more than odd that, in a most ahistorical fashion, Prussia and Austria can become great global powers.  I understand that the players who take these countries deserve to have an equal playing field, but that field needs to be Europe, not South America or the Far East.

I can understand the complaints about luck, but how can this element be removed from the games we love?

The alliance system is nifty, as has been observed by about a gizzillion others.  Yet, only one can win ("one ring to bind them," perhaps).  There's nothing quite like placing a slave revolt tile on your ally, who was leading by one point ahead of you, but now finishes in second place.

I wish the rules were clearer and longer, with more examples.  Why put so much effort into other aspects of the game (basic gameplay, components, etc.) and get "lazy" with the rules?  Why go 1st class in some areas and 3rd class in others?

RATING: 3
I know that Martin Wallace likes dice in games like some people like sugar in tea. Unfortunately the dice in SoE is the equivalent of 6 sugars and half a cup of syrup, except the game is alot more sickly.

It starts off badly with a randon draw of territories to occupy. A nice spread would be a good start but the randomness can cause a huge disparity in starting advantage with 3 counters in one zone effectively cutting a player off from 65% of the board in war 1.

Build a ship and roll a die, attack, roll 2 die, rol a 7 and lose an army regardless, yuk yuk yuk. Sure the dice can be biased with purchased tiles but bad rolls will scupper you into submission regardless before the game even starts. The game will be won on the roll of dice.

For learners the game is tortuously slow, the rules are fiddly and there is endless cross referencing of rules to establish who can do what and when. 

After 3.5 hours we were speeding up but I had lost the will to live early on. Someone had to catch a train and the game was mercifully put to the sword.

A truly nasty experience and never to be repeated. Fine if you love dice and get all excited about rolling good numbers. Crap if you like to drive the game instead iof being a passenger.

RATING: 8.5
Cracking empire building (and empire maintaining) game. It seems to solve many of the aspects of this genre that irritate me: very little downtime, you always seem involved (and everything matters!); plenty of things to do without being overwhelming and much more to the strategy than simply 'stay in the pack until the last turn then bash the leader." Can play long but the time passes quickly. All the mechanisms seem to fit together very well and the game comfortably combines the abstract (bidding for alliances, improvement tiles etc) and concrete (military movement, control and combat). There is some luck insofar as you can win some lucky battles and lose some that should have been walkovers but c'est la guerre. However,I would bet that few games are won by a player who has simply been lucky. And the thematic flavour is spot on. I can understand why some people might find the slavery element an inappropriate, or at least uncomfortable subject for a game that could hardly be described as an accurate simulation of the time, but it does provide a useful extra layer of strategy and period flavour, and to deny the slave trade a role in a game that is inspired by the colonial age would be revisionist in the extreme. (And perhaps it is good to feel uncomfortable.) Mind you, you'll never get me to play a game of Geronimo. Have played with five and six players - might not be quite so much fun with less but am open to persuasion.

RATING: 5.5

RATING: 8
Although it has the illusion of being chaotic, random and brutal, playing Struggle of Empires with experienced players leaves you satiated and satisfied in the civilization, area control and conquest genres. The bidding for alliance mechanic is tense and engaging when competent players vie for the right choice of allies. The tile upgrades provide abilities to mitigate the randomness while providing varied approaches and interesting strategical and tactical decision points.

 Struggles of Empires can be long and haphazard, but the different paths available maintains it's freshness and charm. The consensus is that it scales best with five and I would tend to agree with that opinion. 

This is a gamer's game with a Martin Wallace pedigree that has substantial replayability and gameplay. A keeper!

RATING: 6.5
Okay for a long game that is heavy in politics/diplomacy.  Unrest tokens are a good balancing mechanism; they reduce conflict and over-spending.  (game#1,7p,6hr; game#2,3p,4h).  Personally, I prefer fewer players b/c it reduces time spent on diplomacy and increases randomness b/c fewer country tokens are revealed.

Note to self -- in a 3 player game, it is unwise (certain death) to avoid making alliances for the entire game.  It might save you a bunch of money if opponents are bidding high, but you will lose (and likely get beaten up and kicked out of every country on the board) if you fail to pony up some cash and make an ally for at least one "era" or "turn" of the game.

RATING: N/A
Not Yet Played
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another excellent Martin Wallace game. Area Movement/influence game. Two main alliances decided by auction will fight three wars lasting five or six rounds each.

Player turns involve picking two actions from a menu to build, move, fight, colonize, choose tiles or pass. Battles are abstract but instead of the usual method of totalling strengths, drms and adding the result of a die roll, Martin has you roll two dice and take the difference to which is added various drms to determine the final tally. Ties are a push (no influence markers change hands), but each side loses a unit. A win allows the placement of an influence marker. The placement of influence markers in the various areas will determine the VPs to be gained, which are scored at the end of each war.

The alliance mechanic makes the usual "gang up on the leader" harder to arrange, and the variety of tiles available to provide various advantages adds to the range of decisions players are constantly facing. This game of strategy is fast paced, relatively simple to learn and highly thematic. One of Martin's very best!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
As one of the few Martin Wallace designs that accomodates 2 right out of the box, this was highly sought-after on my part, but hard to find.  Well, not hard to find, but I wasn't willing to spend $50+ on a used copy.  In one of my math trade coups, though, I managed to trade an old, well loved (but never played) copy of Civ for an in-shrink copy of this.  I was very happy.

As a 2 player game, SoE loses only one aspect of the multiplayer game - the alliance mechanism.  To some, this will be a big deal.  For us, not so much.  I can see how the alliance aspect makes the game different, and really expands the burn, but there is a lot of game here, even for 2.  It is like a deeper, more subtle version of AoE3, with all those Wallace interlocking mechanisms we've come to love.  This is one of the best area-control games for two out there (that I've played at least), and all the improvement tiles give the game legs, spice, and variety (all 3!).  Plus, with 2 it is a bit less intense, and certainly a lot faster than with more.  My favorite colonization/conquest game as of now (when ruling out games such as Puerto Rico, which have a more pasted-on theme).

RATING: 8
After London, perhaps my favorite Martin Wallace game. It really gives you the feeling of a war game, but is actually an area-control Euro with a very well-integrated theme. The historical subject is represented in almost every decision you make: I realized this in the very first turn of our very first game, when my first move was to enslave the people of the Carribbean. As horrifying as that is, I am amazed at how the mechanics of the game (combined with one's own desire to win) press players into repeating the same atrocities that were made three- or four-hundred years ago, all in the name of self-advancement.

The historical subject carries through to the rest of the board: the Ottoman empire is notoriously difficult to conquer, Africa is distant and not incredibly lucrative (whereas India is), and the Americas are easier to navigate but are hotly contested, just as they were in the eighteenth-century. I probably won't ask to play this all the time, but it's a fine game with a very well thought-out historical theme. I'm not Martin Wallace's biggest fan, but as far as game design goes, he has yet to fail to impress me (exception: Toledo).

(Rating is after one five-player game.)  Boy, this is a tough game to rate.  So, let me explain my thought process.  SoE is NOT a wargame, it's just a Euro with direct conflict. I had no strategy, other than to get many tiles so I could see how they worked... and my score reflected this.  War is expensive, unrest sucks, and screw assisting alliance members in battle as I lost my unit in the battle exactly half the time.  I did not find luck in the game to be that overpowering an element, but the roll-a-7 rule is nasty and adds risk.  Risk is good... battles should not be entered lightly.  We played a five player game in 3 hours.  I was hoping for seven players, but oh, well.

Bottom line - I want to play again pretty badly, and I have no real criticism of the game, but for some reason I can't rate the game higher than 7.5 right now.  This rating will certainly change after the next game.

RATING: 6
[ImageID=224195 inline] [ImageID=813361 inline] [ImageID=161550 inline] 

[i]1 play. 
6 players.[/i]

[thing=9625]Struggle of Empires[/thing] was my second foray into the world of Martin Wallace "Empire" games.  Although it was certainly an entertaining foray, I couldn't help but find myself making comparisons between this and [thing=30658]Rise of Empires[/thing] -- comparisons that did not always bear favorably for Struggle of Empires. 

Struggle is, fundamentally, an area majority Euro in which players score points for having the first-, second-, and occasionally third-most "control" markers in each country on the board at the end of each of three "wars."  Naturally control is obtained through military conquest, and most of the game is spent carefully accumulating and maneuvering military units to time selective (and expensive) attacks.  The game also contains additional civilization-themed tiles that provide players special powers throughout the game at the cost of the action spent selecting them (a none-too-easy tradeoff, believe me).

Struggle is appropriately tight and unforgiving, and I enjoyed the implementation of a number of the game's traditional Wallace staples: each player's turn comprises exactly two actions, bringing efficiency and timing to the forefront of player strategy; unrest markers for military losses and overspending provide the signature "pain" currency; and a novel alliance mechanic provides the required "Hey, that's clever" moments.  Among experienced players, I have no doubt that the game can provide an extremely entertaining and tense affair, fraught with difficult choices and palpable trade offs.  The core of the game is that good.

Peripheral game features, however, were often much less effective and detracted from my appreciation for the design.  First, the alliance system depends on several rounds of seemingly endless bidding that ran nearly half as long as the rest of the game combined (you know, the parts where you actually [i]do[/i] stuff).  Second, naval movement rules were fiddly and unpredictable, with seemingly little countervailing usefulness.  Third, the civ-tile feature consisted of selecting from a large pool of forty-some-odd small, mostly indistinguishable tiles, a prospect that significantly slowed the game with a few new and a few rusty players at the table.  (For those wondering, our play ran 4-5 hours, including a third war lightening round.)

Although I've previously criticized Rise of Empires for being too abstracted and streamlined (and I did find Struggle more theme rich and oddly charming), at times I found myself longing for its elegance, as well as wishing for secondary points-scoring opportunities once it became clear I wasn't in league with the area-majority big boys (in Rise, this takes the form of city purchases and goods production).  It is clear that Rise of Empires is a refinement of some of Wallace's earlier ideas.

If I could somehow mix Struggle of Empires and Rise of Empires, I think I might find a truly elite empire conquest Euro.  But each in isolation falls short of my ideal, and both typically leave me longing for more.  Moreover, the genre is not one that I have a particular affinity for, so I'm likely less willing to overlook some of the mechanical quibbles than others might be.  Struggle is certainly something I'd play again, but not something I'd be likely to request.  [thing=62219]Dominant Species[/thing] covers so much of the same appealing ground while being neither so fiddly nor so frustrating.  That's good enough for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
My favourite game. It has great replayability because of the random setup, the alliance system and the great variety of improvement tiles. The alliance mechanic is very clever and doesn't generate the hurt feelings produced by games like Diplomacy or Game of Thrones. You know who is on each side and you can always pay to change it. Also much of the combat is against neutral counters.

The range of improvement tiles lets you pursue all sorts of strategies. Limiting your choice to just one each turn is really tough. The unrest counters (taken for losing units or taxing your home country for gold) make the end of the game a real nail-biter if the scores are at all close. After playing this over 20 times, I feel some of the costs of the tiles may be a little off - War Office seems too expensive and Militia too cheap.

Which areas in Europe are adjacent and which can have fleets could have been more clearly shown on the map. The European part of the map is one aspect of the game I'd like to see redone. Why, for instance, call Italy the Mediterranean? It just leads to confusion. The rules leave many unanswered questions. It's worth printing out the FAQ in the files section and keeping a rolled-up copy at hand to swat the rules lawyers with. 

The game length of about 4 hours including rules explanation makes this tricky to get to the table but at least there's not too much downtime since each player only has two actions per turn. 4 is okay for a learning game but it is definitely better with 6 or 7. The alliances with 5 can be very unbalanced. If you end up on the smaller alliance twice (or even all three times) with an ally who doesn't share any of your interests it really will be a struggle.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Struggle of Empires is a remarkably sleek game from Martin Wallace. It is a multiplayer conflict game that takes place over three 'wars'. During each war players take turns in order building units, moving them, and attacking. Another option is to take a special power tile which tend to improve your income, your battle modifiers, and other important dynamics. So far, so typical. What makes the game special is that between each war, there is a series of auctions to determine who is on who's 'alliance'. While members of the two teams don't need to work with each other, they are NOT ALLOWED to attack each other. This makes the auctions incredibly intense as players try to manage turn order, who they don't want attacking them, who they think might help them, and who they want attacking who else. A brilliant mechanism that makes for a great game despite the whole thing clocking in at 6+ hours.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
:sugar: :( :what: :what: :what: :D :) :D The bad aspects, to get them out of the way at the top: this is a supremely dabbler-hostile game. Mechanically quite simple compared to just about every war-themed game I know, but with a lot of information overload up front. It demands attention from its players. It's a bit long (it's eminently possible to play in under three hours -- I've done it -- but it takes buy-in from the players at the table). It more or less requires a minimum of five players, and getting four to six willing opponents for this sort of game is not an everyday event for me. It's dependent on the players at the table getting into the spirit of the thing -- interaction is everything. And you can get screwed a bit by luck of the dice, though that tends to balance out over the long haul.

Having said that, I would like to advance the hypothesis that SoE is not only a stockholding game masquerading as a wargame, a la Imperial, but that SoE is a [i]better[/i] stockholding game masquerading as a wargame than Imperial is, with the control tokens serving as your portfolio. 

And I like Imperial quite a bit.

At game start, everyone is more or less equal, though the random draw of a few tokens ensures a variable setup game-to-game. (The downside, such as it is, of this variability is that what little fidelity to historicity the game has goes utterly by the wayside the second you gaze upon the fertile plantations of the Russian West Indies.) The canny player is on the lookout for opportunities to score cheap points, and the ways in which the multi-polar alliance auction at the start of each of the three wars can be manipulated are both intriguing and hard to appreciate when you first start -- the auction determines both turn order (in which both the first and last positions have some merit depending on the board position) and the alliance structure. Is it worth precious gold to go early to grab that fat colonization chit in the Caribbean? Maybe, but if you ensure you go first, you will have less control over who your allies and enemies are, and everyone will get one shot to play off your moves in Round 5. The tiles, too, are a source of joy and angst in equal measure. The more you grab, the stronger your position will be, but the more actions you are sacrificing, and actions are precious and scarce.

SoE is not to everyone's taste. It's too much wargame for Europhiles and too much Euro for the grogs, and there's a tremendous amount of information to drink in. But it hits a sweet spot for me and I am quite fond of it.

RATING: 8
I really didn't expect to like this game as much as I did given all the talk about the randomness of the dice. In reality, luck plays a lesser role (roll?) in the combat of this game than it does in Princes of the Ren, as the "roll two and the difference is the result mechanism" tends to tighten the distribution up more than a straight roll 1 die, and add it to the starting total. That isn't to say that there isn't a significant random aspect in other parts of the game (initial seeding, native resistence, rolls to see if you lose ships going across the pond, &c.) but I think there is enough control via the special power chits to make it not only serviceable but very good even for a control freak like me.

Interesting game in any case, a hybrid of sorts between a traditional area influence game and an empire building one with a metric tonne of special chits to tailor your strategy with. I had to catch myself several times in the game trying to attack from adjacent territories with units because the presentation was pure Risk, but the gameplay is most definitely not in any way, shape, or form. Very tight resources as seems to be standard for Wallace games too...never enough population, spare unrest, or money for that matter. The alliance system is also wonderful, and it saved my proverbial bacon in many situations. Overall, really, really high quality...grafts an area influence scoring system and Euro tightness onto an American style conquest game and makes it not only work but shine. Definitely prefer playing this with odd player counts, and this is one of the few games that I think actually scales well to 7. 5 is also very good.

RATING: 9
After struggling to get people in my game group to play it ("too long", "too complex", etc.) and finally getting to play this excellent game, I've realized that German games have had the same effect on gamers as MTV has had on, well, everything.  I think gamers have been spoiled by fancy graphics/visuals and short play times and as a result attention spans for richer, longer games seem to be on the decline.  After our first test game, at least three people thought it was too long and never wanted to play it again.  Which is a shame, because SoE is a magisterial, elegant empire-building game very much in the mold of classic Avalon Hill titles like Age of Renaissance and Civilization seasoned with some of the usual Eurogame mechanics (area control in particular).  Make no mistake, this is not a 90 minute game by any stretch of the imagination but because the game has time to develop there is a level of depth, development, and strategy that is much more rewarding to serious players than most games we've seen come out in the past few years.  Rules-wise, the game isn't any more complex than Puerto Rico and once you get past the  (as usual for Warfrog) horrible rule book you'll find a game that will be well worth revisiting long after your copy of Goa has been traded off.  This is a good one, buy it while it's around or pay out the nose for it later on when it's OOP and everyone realizes they missed a winner.

$34 Amazon
Struggle of Empires recreates the various wars fought between the European powers as they attempted to become the dominant force in Europe and the rest of the world during the eighteenth century. Build armies and fleets, make alliances, establish colonies, improve your economy, and ultimately wage war to expand your empire. Be careful, though, as a profligate country can end up being consumed by revolution.

RATING: 9
A great conquest game. There are many different options to take with your empire. Just the perfect amount of randomness for this type of game. Alliances are fickle, but in the end it's basically every man for himself. Includes many interesting mechanisms, including auction for turn order (and alliances), taxing for money and causing unrest among population, spending population to build armies, paying upkeep for armies, and combat rolls using the spread of dice outcomes. Easy to learn, but difficult to master, it seems.  Probably better, the more players participating. Just the right amount of negotiation in a game, and I love how the alliances can change over the course of the game. Overall, an excellent psuedo-wargame with quite a bit of player interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Superb, if long, game where players assume control of a major power in the 18th century.  The game lasts 3 wars (turns) during which players take 5 or 6 rounds of  two actions.  Actions include building troops/fleets, moving, colonising, fighting or acquiring upgrade/alliance/company tiles.  These tiles create a lot of variety and interest in the way they interact.  After each war an El Grande like scoring takes place over several areas of the board - the Americas, Asia, and Europe.  Scoring is based on the presence of your control markers in these regions - these are placed by defeating the native states, other players present, or by colonising the regions.  A very clean game to play, probably around 3 hours in length once everyone is up to speed.  Several nice things going on - the alliance/turn order bidding system is very clever, and I love the unlimited bankroll/Unrest system (along with the consequences if it's abused!).  This game has made an excellent early impression.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Amazing diplomatic wargame, and if feels like the game that Martin Wallace has been building up to for quite some time. The only game that is in the same ballpark is Dune. 
 
For a wargame, it uses an awful lot of classic German mechanisms: scarce action points, auctions for turn order, area-control scoring, and so on. One clever breakthrough is the dice convention, to use the difference of dice to generate a number. But the real crowning beauty is the alliances: every turn you auction off the partnerships and rivalries that determine which players will be allies. Or maybe alliegence is too strong a term, more like mutual non-aggression pacts.
 
In any case, the design is a thing of beauty and the only caveat is the massive time commitment to enjoy this rare gem of a game. No matter how well-versed the players, this is a long long long gruelling experience that limits its ability to hit the table.

RATING: 9
I have played Struggle of Empires once recently. We were 7 players. We only played 2 of the 3 wars since someone had to leave. It was enough to get our feet wet with the game rules.

Every player was new to Struggle of Empires except one which just played once before. So with newbies, downtime was to be expected. 

At first I wasn't much into the game. Once the game made some progress, slowly but surely I was starting to warm up to the game. At the end of the second war when we decided to stop, I was very happy with the overall game experience. I would want to play the game again sometime soon. Looking forward to my second game.

UPDATE: Struggle of Empires is an interesting game which I would like to try again, though maybe with 5 players instead of 7 players. There was a very long downtime during turns since we were all newbies when we played Struggle of Empires. I suppose that the game would play faster with experience knowing the game rules.

Also, with 5 players, Struggle of Empires should be more interesting when bidding for force alliances. In my opinion, players would spend more money than in a 7 player game. Especially for the 5th player in order who gets a 3 vs. 2 advantage. 

Example, having a oppenent which is right at your door step and is stronger than you, you would want to net to have him in your alliance so that he would attack you the next turn.

RATING: 2
Pretty impressive.  The alliance auction in SoE is actually even better than it appears at first glance.  It is simple to understand the idea, that you want to pay to get a player who might attack you on your side.  It takes a play to understand that mitigating almost all of the random chance in battle is possible if you can create board situations where it is clearly in another players' interest to join you.  This requires that you spot these opportunities before they are obvious, AND that you arrange the alliances to take advantage of them (or, similarly, to neutralize others doing it to you).

The luck in this game is therefore mostly manageable.  The rolls for sea voyages are a bit more problematic, because they cannot be well mitigated, except by taking Navigation tiles, and there aren't enough rolls to not feel a little capricious.

All things aside, I would probably rate this an 8.5 or even a 9 because it is hella fun (although the dealmaking starts to get weird in the last war). But I have rated it in line with my desire to play because of the shocking amoral casualness of turning the players into explicit Transatlantic slave traders.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The much lauded alliance system is brilliant and is the main attraction here. Scoring is done via the mundane process of area majority, but it gives you something to fight about. I just wish there was another mechanism to score points (build Wonders, maybe? I'm joking). There are two big problems that hang me up in this game. 

First is that there are choke points in the game that drag the flow to a halt, especially with my group. The bidding takes way too long to conduct. Oh, how I wish it was a once-around with the start player getting last bid. The game drags with too many "whose bid is it?" moments and rotations around the table. Complicating matters is that my group takes forever to negotiate; we have point, counterpoint, counter to the counter, and then final word, summary, parting shot, and one more thing. [i]Whose bid is it?[/i]

The second major problem is that it's not uncommon for players to be effectively out of it by the end of the first war, and certainly a good bit are out if it by the second such that the game is down to a few key players on stage with everyone else watching. I’m all for dramatic battle results due to dice, but here the penalty for losing a battle you had prepared heavily for are so devastating in terms of the actions you had taken and the ones you’ll need to take to fix it that it ends up being de facto player elimination. It's not uncommon for players to relegated to spoilers mid-way through. The third war turns into players identifying who they think the leader is -- [i]Him! No, it's him![/i] -- and attacking that person/those people. The winner will be one of the few people in position at the end of the second war, everyone else is reduced to spoiler if not already.

This comment sounds harsher than I intended, I'm just disappointed I'm not among the ranks of those who are delighted by this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I am not totally secure with my rating here.  I feel as this game has a lot to offer, but after post game conversations and thought I don't know if it really does.  The too few actions to do what you want does add tension, but it also makes the action stumble along.  All actions seem to progress at snails pace allowing for very few surprises in battle, and most of the time basically just gives your opponenet a heads up to your plans.  This causes a lot of turtling.  A lot of the rules and mechanics feel gamey, and in contrast to the theme.  The alliance auction although clever makes little sense when applied to the idea of nations at war.  Why would a pummeled nation ally with it's pummeler?  This really plays as an area control game more than a war title as it is a game of efficently gathering VPs rather than conquests of nations.  The idea that territories are shared, and the way ties are resolved all pull away from any real sense that you are engaged in battle.  Another issue is with the cards.  The cards that seem most powerful are limited to one or two, significantly aiding the first players in turn order during the first war.  I'm just not convinced there are any real decisions here.  I had fun yes, it felt tense, yes, but really not sure if that is just a mirage set up by limiting actions.  Will have to play again.

UPDATE: No interest to play again

RATING: 2
Very rarely does every member of our group come to a complete agreement regarding a game the first time we play it. This, however, has happened with Struggle of Empires. This is a game gone all bad due to the huge element of dice rolling involved. It does not matter what you plan or decide, winners and loser alike are solely determined by the roll of the die. This is not a bad thing in a game like Formula De (which I believe still has a lower element of luck than this game), but to me (and apparently all others in my group) is unacceptable in a game from Martin Wallace. The worst thing is that this could have been a great game, what with the alliance mechanisms, improvement tiles, etc. However, if we want a random luckfest, we’ll play something that is simple and fun, and not a game that takes itself seriously but should never have introduced such a high element of luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Is this Risk advanced? I enjoyed this game quite a bit. It has a good combination of wargame, managing limited resources within your empire, special advancements, and negotiation. It may very well increase in rating with more plays. The question is whether it is possible to sufficiently mitigate the randomness of dice rolling by negotiating. The first play was clearly a learning experience and I rolled terribly losing almost every battle despite reasonable odds in my favor. Unlike other games that use dice modifiers, it seems very difficult to substantially reduce the randomness of the rolls without good political negotiation and alliances. Essentially negotiating beneficial alliances seems much more important than having an extremely strong military presence. 

Rating increasing slightly with second play. Clearly the key to this game is the alliance system and competent negotiation to not only secure crucial modifier bonuses, but also to deal with other alliance formed on the board. Greatly enjoying this game.

(extra copy for trade, new in shrink)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]Struggle of Empires[/b] [i]Martin Wallace 2005[/i] 
Although it is not a hard game, it has a lot of chrome in the rules as well as many unclear situations that must be solved with a FAQ. Its subtle and is hard to know the correct way to play it. Struggle of Empires creates a web of alliances that must be wisely used in the wars during the game flow. It is a very psicologycal game, in which temperate reactions against betrayal or other players actions is often the key to succed. Never hurt another player if you are not going to benefit yourself, break the alliances in the important moments, keep an eye on these silent players who are really dangerous if they manouvre without oposition... just an amazing experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I've played twice now (with six and with four), and I have to admit that I'm rather disappointed.  First of all, the game is very long - too long for a game with such overwhelming luck and so much arbitrary whacking among the players.  I was very surprised that the game is such a diplomatic bash-your-neighbor free-for-all - not quite what I expected.  I also was surprised by the degree that the game turned on the luck of the dice.  A bad roll or two at just the wrong time can put you completely out of the game, with no chance to recover.  Every unit, control marker, and action is precious, and it's much too easy to lose many of them due to some capricious die rolling.  Consider the following scenario: 

It's nearing the end of the first war.  I wish to attack the Ottoman Empire, where two players have one control marker each, with no neutral armies present.  I choose the weaker of the two opponents, who has just one army in the territory, one fleet in the Mediterranean, and no army superiority.  I have two armies, two fleets, army superiority, and the Ottoman Empire alliance tile.  Overwhelming forces.  I choose to use the alliance tile to aid in the naval battle, since the +1 naval support bonus will all but guarantee victory in the land battle.  I roll 4/3 = 1 + 2 for fleets + 1 for alliance = 4.  My opponent rolls 5/1 = 4 + 1 for his fleet = 5.  I lose the naval battle and the ensuing support bonus, lose both of my fleets, and gain two unrest.  Okay, that's very bad for me, but at least I still have the advantage in the land battle.  I roll 3/4 = 1 + 2 for armies + 1 for army training = 4.  My opponent rolls 3/6 = 3 + 1 for his army + 1 for naval support = 5.  I lose the battle, lose both of my armies, and gain two more unrest.  My opponent will score four points that he shouldn't have scored for the territory, while I won't score the four that I should have.  That's an eight-point swing in victory points and a two-point swing in income - even more of both if he can hold his control for future wars.  I've lost four units, which represents four actions and four population (remember, population is also income!).  That's probably about half of my entire military force, and so I'm liable to be hammered and hindered everywhere on the board, not just in the Ottoman Empire.  Additionally, the extra four unrest could cause me a loss of seven more victory points at the end of the game.  I may need two additional actions to get rid of it (governemnt reform tiles).  In short, I'm in a hopeless position with three quarters of the game left to be played.  That stinks.  It's not sour grapes, either, since I've won once and come second once in my two sessions. 

There are some great ideas in this game, but overall it just doesn't pass muster.  I loved Liberte and Age of Steam, and liked Princes of the Renaissance, but I think I'm finished with Struggle of Empires.

RATING: 6.5
Three plays, each with four players, I think. The game has two really cool features, which are the alliance mechanism and the "character" of the different regions dictated by the neutral control markers that are turned over each round. It's also kind of neat in that it's often a better idea for players to settle into detentes with each other and be concerned with the balance of power than it is to try to dominate the board outright. Otherwise, though, it's pretty much the ol' push-each-other-around-and-score-majorities type of thing. There is a large array of tiles that one can buy, but these end up being less interesting than they look, since most of them just allow the player extra resources or the ability to pack extra actions into his turn and so don't really affect overall strategy much. Additionally, the need for constant opportunism and the need to spread yourself as thinly as possible makes for a game that is both extremely demanding and paranoia-inducing. Overall I think it's a solid game that *could* be fun with the right group, but even so it's not one I'm especially enthusiastic about.

RATING: 8
An excellent game. Great subtleties in tactics and choosing alliances. From my experiences an early leader sets themself up as a target so it's often an advantage to be in the chasing pack come the end of the 2nd war, providing the leader isn't too far ahead.

The rules aren't all that complicated but there are a lot of different tiles to learn and for a beginner it can be quite daunting knowing what action to take.

RATING: 8.99999
Wonderfully difficult choices based on the myriad tile uses.

The elegantly simple bidding mechanism for Alliance position is sheer genius BUT this game has the typical Kingmaker/Bash the Leader problem inherant in most multi-player wargames.

Two possible Play Balance mechanisms would be to have players bid for nations initially and then each turn have the player in last place point-wise rather than positionally at the end of the current war be first (followed in ascending order) to choose potential Alliances for the next war. Small advantages but this game needs SOMETHING to prevent or slowdown runaway leaders who come about due to the initial placement.

RATING: 6
MdM

Es un 4X que funciona en base a mayorias. se juega en un mapa de europa y ciertas partes del mundo, y el objetivo es ser el mejor imperio (el que mas conquiste, basicamente). 

La premisa esta bien, suena un poco a risk. el desarrollo es bastante mas complejo. tiene partes en plan eurogame y ir desarrollando unidades militares (que consigues mediante mayorias en territorios conquistados) y expandiendo con ellas tus dominios. 

El gran punto del juego es el sistema de alianzas. cambia de ronda a ronda y basicamente crea dos "superbandos", de modo que si coincides en un lugar del mapa con alguien con quien estas aliado, no te peleas con el, y en caso contrario, si. Esto es lo mejor del juego sin ninguna duda, y lo que le da una gran diferencia respecto a cualquier otro juego del genero. 

El resto... es bastante farragoso, y el juego es notoriamente mediocre en diseño y calidades materiales. mencion especial a las unidades militares, que bien podrian pasar por un juego de los 80s. 

Es uno de esos juegos que es un clasico, pero que no necesariamente es una experiencia que quieras repetir. xD

RATING: 7.5
Martin Wallace's best game I've played.  Combination area control, with risk-like combat, layered with a very cool alliance mechanism.  The game uses lots of variable player power tiles you can buy.  Like a lot of martin wallace games, there is a debt mechanism and debt is encouraged.  The alliance mechanism is probably the best mechanic.  I wish someone would steal it and use it in a game that doesn't involve 4 hours of risk plus area majority, which to be honest isn't a very interesting exercise.  The alliance mechanism drives the game.  Its also an interesting way to deal with kingmaking/player elimination/etc., because the people in the alliance act in the alliance's best interest.  

The negatives are game length and end game diplomacy that can sometimes drive the winner of the game.  Its a four hour game and it feels a little long for what it is.  If they could fit this game into a 2 or 2.5 hour window it would be incredible.  The end game diplomacy can get a little silly as the 2 or 3 players in contention for the win beg for help from the players that aren't in the lead.  "He is going to win.  No I'm not, he is!"  Its a little crazy that a four hour long euro-wargame hybrid can come down to this type of bickering.  I enjoy social interaction in games, but it feels odd here because it happens after 3 hours of cold number crunching through battles and area majority stuff.

Overall, a very good game but not one I'm interested in owning.

RATING: 9
Resources (money and population) are agonizingly tight in this game. The game rewards people who find a way to do more with less. What I like about the scarcity of resources is that it increases the risk-taking. There is an incentive to spread yourself a bit thin if you can because you can trust that it is probably too costly for your opponent to attack you here or there. But you never know: they might just decide to spend the coin and take the unrest to attack you anyway. But if you shore up your defenses in anticipation of an attack, you don't have the resources to expand. That agony provides the perfect motivation for, and premium on, the diplomacy/alliances mechanic, which makes for a very exciting gaming experience.

RATING: 7.5
This is yet a another winner from Martin Wallace.

He's taken a big, sloppy dicefest game, tightened up the actions and the funds (a lot!), and then added a no-man's line in the sand with the concept of unrest.

It still has some of the feeling of a Risk, but every loss stings because of unrest, and even some wins.

It's not a quick game, but you don't notice the time much once you get started- or at least only that there's not enough time or money to do what you want to with your Empire.

It doesn't pretend to follow history, but the need to watch your expenses while maximizing your gains makes it a real gamer's game, despite it's sloppy roots.

Even on the first play, this is (at least) a 7.5 - no doubt in my mind.

RATING: 8
Wow... this has to be Martin Wallace's perfect empire building game.

The game mechanics and finances are so tight, with battles so costly and bloody, that the game conveys an epic feel of "struggle" through three "wars". It is similar in feel and scope to Age of Renaissance or Rise of Empires, but without the civilization aspect of it. It is more of a war game, with euro concepts like area control and bidding, along with an Ameritrash "fog of war" feel through its simple dice combat system.  

The alliance bidding mechanic is simply brilliant, with a "game within a game", where players propose combatant pairings, which ultimately form alliances as well as the turn order for a given war.

Players only have two actions per each of the six rounds that make up a war. There is a limited menu of actions available, but great flexibility about where, who, and what, much like in Railroad Tycoon. Like RRT, there are a number of tiles that can be picked up as an action (but you can only pick up one per round), similar to the operation cards, but these are all out. Some are temporary, signifying alliances with the locals for one war, while others are permanent, giving additional income or combat capabilities.

There is also the interesting "unrest" counters that are dished out for every military unit lost in combat. They are similar to the "misery" in AoR, or like corruption in Cleopatra and the Architects. 20x unrest, and your empire collapses. Having more unrest than your opponents will cost serious points at the end of the game. However, generating unrest is a crucial way of generating income, which is extremely hard to come by.

This has all the hallmarks of a great Wallace game. Simple mechanics, limited set of actions, but great flexibility on application, which results in a myriad of agonizing choices and strategies.

After one play, it seems like it will scale nicely, but is optimum with an odd number of players, as this will make the alliance bidding part of the game tension-filled and entertaining!

In comparison with other "empire" games:
1) It plays in a shorter amount of time than AoR (3 vs 6 hours);
2) The overall mechanics seem to blend more naturally than the "stock market/rondel" aspect of Imperial and Imperial 2030;
3) It is more focused on empire building through military action than Wallace's later attempt in Rise of Empires, which tries to mix some civilization/culture aspect, but is essentially also an area control game (which uses a similar scoring system).

I like it. I can see why it is so revered in some gaming circles. I eagerly look forward to more plays. 

Update (07/04/11): We replaced the cardboard military unit counters with detailed plastic miniatures from Viktory II from Morrison Games. There are enough units in Viktory II (the 8-player set) to replace all the cardboard unit counters. Plus, the colors of the plastic units match the colors of the 7 nations! As the [b][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/1031180/struggle-of-empires]image[/url][/b] shows, this gives the game a more vibrant 3D feel. After this, there is no going back to cardboard units.

Update (01/20/12): I just finished reading the rules of my own copy, which BTW are well written. Despite the rules density, I was surprised to see how simple the game is in terms of mechanics. Although there are a limited set of actions a player can take, it is how those various simple elements interact that makes the game sophisticated and engrossingly complex. At first glance, I thought the alliance auction mechanism would be too constraining, for what I thought was an empire building game. It became immediately apparent that without the alliance mechanism, the game would quickly fall into chaos, due to the openness of the board--there are no "fronts" per se, like an area control/conquest game such as Risk or Twilight Imperium (especially the 1st and 2nd editions), since there is fluidity of movement to almost everywhere on the board. Alliances allow each player to [i]focus[/i] their extremely limited number of units and resources (when compared to Risk or TI) on achieving specific objectives, instead of wasting it on defending their "perimeter"--what perimeter? Consolidation then becomes a matter of shrewdly forming alliances that force enemies to cooperate, while also forcing them to eat away at their friends. It seems counter-intuitive at first, but the alliance mechanism brilliantly simulates the chaotic, seemingly schizophrenic, but ultimately [i]self-serving[/i] nature of constantly changing alliances of the period. Depending on how the alliances form, this may remove the inhibitions of timid and cautious players, while constrain the vengeful tendencies of players who hold a grudge--the game is "forcing" you to attack; it's nothing personal. The alliances can also be used by the crafty player to passively-aggressively and opportunistically achieve their long-term goals, provided they are willing to pay the price in gold to "buy" this leverage. 

In this manner, SoE compares favorably with Shogun, rather than Risk, where it is about consolidation, rather than expansion, exploiting opportunities subtly, rather than through sheer force. There is admittedly a lot going on in the shifting alliances, under the seemingly simple and clean mechanisms. It is not a game of conquest.

As for the seemingly random nature of combat, after contemplating this, I am more comfortable with the dice rolling. Waging battles and wars was expensive, and fraught with peril. The game models this unpredictability well, but also offers enough ways to mitigate the randomness.

RATING: 7
Interesting game about the quest for dominance among 18th century European empires.  Unfortunately, the game starts off on the wrong foot with an unforgivable design decision: random starting locations.  Thus, right off the bat, the British can control Poland while the Russians control Virginia and the Austrians dominate South America.  All this before the game even starts.  The lack of predetermined setups is a major omission.  Most of the game's mechanics work pretty well, although I found the celebrated alliance bidding system boring and tedious.  I can see how fans of auction games would like it.  I really like the simple yet effective combat system and the way that all of the different tiles affect play.  There are many strategic options available, from European vs colonial to militaristic vs economic; although again, much of this will be forced upon you by the random setup.  Like most multi-player games, this game has a serious smear the leader problem, so it probably works best with two players taking multiple empires.  Overall, it's a very solid design, but the random setup and the usual multi-player game problems keep me from getting too excited about it.

Area control/empire building game set in the 18th century. The various special ability tiles are also well thought out and work really well. Apart from that, I really didn't like it.

RATING: 5
2017-07-30

Initial Rating: 6.0 (Jul 2017)

Martin Wallace is one of my favorite game designers. I tend to like his older stuff more so I was very keen to play Struggle of Empires. Before playing the game, I didn’t know (remember) it has any relation to Conquest of the Empire.

The rules seem complex at first but they aren’t hard to teach once you understand them, however, the rulebook doesn’t do a good job at teaching them, so we had many questions during the game. This made the game a lot longer. Playtime could be around 4-5 hours if all players are familiar with the rules. With more than 4 players , it has too much downtime for me, however, the game really shines with 6 or more players :/.

The theme is ok.

The game components are functional, but as is usual from Wallace´s games, they aren’t very pretty. The same applies to the art. The game map isn’t very functional as it appear to have some boundaries that aren’t really boundaries in the rules :/.

Struggle of Empires is a area control game with some neat mechanisms. The alliance system works very well and is the main feature of the game. You can’t directly attack your ally and you can fight together if both players wish to do so. The alliance system also decides turn order. The game´s decisions aren’t simple or trivial. The first part of each turn is to bid for alliances, this decision is very tense and fun as money is tight so choosing the right amount of money and alliance combination isn’t obvious. Each turn you have only two actions (attack, build a unit, move, buy a development, colonize, enslave population, pass) so it isn’t easy to choose the best one, there are many considerations. I really like this part of the game.

There is considerable luck with the battles and the movement to the colonies, but I feel there are ways to mitigate it, so it doesn’t bother me much. However, a bad roll can really hose you at a critical moment. I´m not sure how I feel about the random setup, here, luck decides everything and there is no way to mitigate it.

My big issue with the game is the blatant kingmaking it promotes (one of the worst sins a game can commit). In our game, I decimated a player in the second year, he was out of the game, but in the third year he decided to help another player just to spite me, that’s ok, it’s part of life, but what I really loathe is the way the game allowed him to hinder the rest of us. In the alliance phase he borrowed a lot of money to decide the alliances and he had access to unlimited money. As he couldn’t win, he didn’t care about the penalties so he decided the alliance, even though he had a lot less money than the rest of us. Until this point, I was enjoying the game, after this happened I lost all interest in it and I only finished the game due to courtesy to other players. I didn’t care about the end result. It is hard to understand why Wallace would permit such abuse of the money system, but, reading the rules, it seems we played it correctly (I wasn’t the player who read the rules but I parsed them while we played it, so perhaps I missed something). This is my main issue with the game.

Another consideration, I would have liked the game very much a couple of years ago, but I have changed a lot during this time. I no longer enjoy “dudes on a map” wargame style of games and its derivatives so my desire to play this kind of game was and is very low. I don’t like games in which bash the perceived leader is a constant.

Bottom line, Struggle of Empires can be a good game in its genre, it has hard decisions and lots of tension during its gameplay, but it has a fatal flaw for me, it promotes a blatant kingmaking with its money system and I heavily dislike this feature of the game. It has a high luck factor which I dislike in long games, however there are way to, somewhat, mitigate it. Its best feature is the alliance system which is brilliant. The downtime is too much for me. I will sell the game ASAP. I prefer to play Princes of the Renaissance. I´m disappointed by Struggle of Empires.

Current Rating: 5.0

RATING: 7
Struggle of Empires does so many things right - a large variety of special powers, opposed combat rolls that somehow don't feel as random as they could, and an alliance system that is both perfectly thematic and perfectly executed mechanically.

I do wish it were more Civ-like in the victory point scoring; area control is just too stressful for me.  But it is what it is.  It at the very least belongs with El Grande and Acquire on the list of area control games worth playing despite the mechanic.  Might be worthy of a 9 after another play or three, but I'm slotting it at 8 because that's where it's more likely to fall for me through no fault of its own, just because it's area control.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Struggle of Empires is Martin Wallace's biggest game in terms of scope - players are colonial powers vying for control of distant colonies and weak European states. It plays up to 7, a huge and unusual number for a game of this strategic depth. At its heart, the game is majority control - the players try to have a majority of control markers in each area in order to score the maximum points at the end of each war. A game consists of 3 wars and each war consists of 5 action rounds. For a majority game, the level of conflict is very high. Players cannot simply place control markers from their stock into a region, they either have to defeat native armies, or the armies of other players to increase their control. Some areas can be colonized or enslaved, but that is the only way to get a control marker without fighting for it. And fighting is expensive. It costs population (and thus income) to produce units. You need to pay money directly in order to fight. And any units lost in battle creates unrest among your people, which can count against your score at the end of the game. Money is tight, but you can always get it at the cost of further unrest...

There are lots of options. Players can form alliances with the native populations to help them. Players can form companies in areas where they have control in order to generate income. Also, there are numerous improvement tiles that can increase an empire's capacity to fight, generate soldiers and navies more cheaply, reduce unrest, and generate additional income. It all can be a bit overwhelming the first time playing, but despite the way Wallace has jammed so many different mechanics into a single game (majority control, card drafting, economics, fighting, special powers...) it comes together beautifully and, miraculously, it all works.

Flaws? The game does use dice for combat and for the risks of transporting troops across the vast oceans, but Wallace has built in mechanics in order to minimize the extreme results that dice sometimes provide. Rather than a straight die roll for combat, two dice are rolled and the difference between them is the base result added to your army's strength. This results in lots of zeros and ones, making the battles frequently close so that army strength is a greater factor than dice rolls. Still, stronger armies do lose. There are tiles, though, that mitigate these risks. I don't think the dice hurt the game because of all the opportunities provided to improve or change results. One thing that I do think is an issue is that players who are significantly behind after the 2nd War really have no chance to catch up. This leads to them being able to choose which leaders to hurt with no benefit to themselves. A problem that exists with many interactive multiplayer games.

I haven't even gotten to the best part! The one absolutely brilliant part of the game is the alliance system. At the beginning of each war, players bid money in order to dictate, 2 empires at a time, who will be allied with whom. In each war there are one of two alliances that every empire falls into and you are not allowed to directly attack an ally. So at the beginning of each war, you need to decide who you want to be allied with and how much you are willing to pay to make it happen. It's generally best to be allied with your enemies and a war with your friends in order to protect your interests. Although allies are not obligated to help each other, allies are allowed to fight for one another - if they can be convinced to do so. Money can be freely exchanged between players for a number of purposes - to convince an ally to help you in an attack or defense, extort money from an enemy with the threat of force, pay an enemy to attack one of your allies. The alliance system is a truly inspired piece of game design.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
