RATING: 8
Struggle of Empires models itself on El Grande, yet somehow it emerges from that game's considerable shadow. Each nation begins with some funds and a moderately sized population from which to train armies. Waging war costs money, money requires taxes, taxes create political unrest, and political unrest can undermine your position at the end of the game. On their turn, players may augment their strategies by selecting from a pool of available tiles. For example, a player in India may take an East India Company tile to increase funds. Another player may select a tile that denotes advanced training for armies or innovation in banking. The pool of strategy tiles helps vary the approaches taken from game to game. Players also participate in alliances that are valid for a full third of the game (each round of the game represents a cross-continental war). In a five player game, three players will be one one side, and two will be on another. Alliances do not require players to actively help one another, but they do make certain targets off limits, preventing the game from being an outright free-for-all. Alliances are pay-to-play, so you may find your coffers bare after buying off an aggressor or sowing discord between two neighboring powers. The alliances are the stand-out feature of Struggle of Empires.

Update 10-18-09: Second play several months later. Masterpiece of a game. 

Update: 12-17-09: A 9 may have been over-enthusiastic. The alliance system is great, and the strategy-tile drafting really forks players down different paths, but in the second half of the game, amassing forces and attacking the leader(s) can seem like a real chore. By contrast, El Grande always remains quite fluid and there are always clever options to take. Here, when two players have to double-up on army-building to outpace an entrenched leader, and then slowly grind him down - it's just not the most satisfying way to close out the game. Also, [i]in theory[/i] perhaps the dice are rolled often enough for luck to even out, but this game seems to consistently frustrate players with all kinds of low-odds losses (1/36 ish). It could be that I have no idea what the real odds are when you attack with +1 or +2. Even if I'm misunderstanding that, the two-action limit makes it very hard to amass forces without the defender matching or even outpacing you with forts.

RATING: 6
Traded away 18/11/2009
---
What a difference between my first and my second play (this time with 7 players). All the clever mechanisms which enthused me on first play, paled when the problems of this game showed up. The game was slow killing any atmosphere, uneven distributions in starting positions and dice rolls did their part to take players (ok, include me here) out of the running and a final alliance round gone wrong led to a runaway leader. The alternative would have been a solid game of leaderbashing with a nearly random winner out of the two or three next players. For the amount of rules, the supposedly many strategic options and length of play both possibilities are no acceptable outcome. 6/10 is the average of my both plays (4/10 for the second game) although I'm not sure if I'll ever find the mood again to invest the needed effort in this game when there are so many more satisfying games to be played.  
---
What looks confusing at first comes together real smooth. It features two innovative mechanisms: forced alliances by bidding and the unrest system as economic core of the game. Each turn the targets change depending on the current alliances and keep the game open until the end. Luck plays a big part in attacks but there's much more to consider than war and a bad strategy won't win even with the biggest luck. Definitely want to play again but I wouldn't play it with less than 5 players. Below that number the alliance mechanism won't live up to its potential.

RATING: 9
As far as multi-player war games go, this is the best yet. Limited ability to act is what causes us that special Martin Wallace style of pain in this game. You just can't accomplish everything you'd like. Action limits, population limits, money limits, even the alliance system limits your ability to attack when and where you'd like. Then again, everyone is in the same boat, so the game is about being more efficient than your opponents. The improvement tiles offer a multitude of strategies. The theme captures the era fairly well. A bit long for your average Eurogamer, especially when learning. Experienced players should be able to complete the game well within everyone's attention span: 30 minutes per players seems a reasonable guideline, and worth every minute.

RATING: 10
Well... simply put this is the best multi-player wargame I've seen.  I know many players would characterize this as a "strategy game" instead of a wargame, but I feel differently  The reason for that is because the came DEMANDS conflict.

The game is a master piece of design.  Well balanced, and absolutely rich in the depth of game experience.  The bidding for Alliances and turn order is so simple, yet so sublime.

What would I change?  
-I'd love the rulebook to be better organized, with some additional clarity.
-I'd love to see the game with super production values, i.e. wooden pieces shaped like soldiers, ships and forts.

Despite these desires I still give this game a 10 because it plays so well.  I will not pass up a chance to play it.

RATING: 10
Sometimes I feel like this game's most boring and tireless promoter. It's a great empire-building game with a perfect balance of depth and ease of play; it can run a little long for some people, but the downtime is interesting enough, particularly with 5-6 players. Some people think the most original element of this game is the alliance mechanism, in which you bid over who you're allowed to attack. Personally, I think the best thing about Struggle of Empires is that while it doesn't have too terribly many measurements of success to keep track of, it has a wide range of tiles that allow you to really customize your strategy. There is no single obvious route to victory. [Editor: I've never come close to winning without Banking, though...] The dice mechanism is pretty innovative (and a leetle homage to Settlers, perhaps?). Manages to be on-theme despite the somewhat plain board. While it's quite complex due to the choice of tiles, the rules fit onto three or four pages.  

Since there's no game I'd rather play, and there's no game that would even give me pause if I had to choose between the two, this is among only two or three 10s I can think of, but of those, it's by far the best.

RATING: 7
Based on 3.5 plays.  So far SoE is my favorite take on the empire-building genre.  The forced alliance system is brilliant, the area control is elegant, and the combat resolution is quick (albeit a bit random).  Still, it's not perfect.  The length of play is a bit long, and some of the rules (movement in particular) are clunky.  My biggest beef with the game is the quality of the components.  The cardboard chits really should be wooden bits, the tiles ought to be full-blown cards, and the board is far too small.    Still, the gameplay is strong enough for me to favor SoE over its glitzier cousin, Conquest of the Empire.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Solid longer, multi-player wargame.
Simpler than it looks with all the chits out.

You own Control Points in the various geographic regions.  Having presence in a region gets you victory points.  You want to gain control of other player's to expand, but the beginning of each of the 3 wars involves an auction that forces an alliance between certain players.  You cannot attack allies, and you're not forced to attack non-allies.  So what trusts you built up over one war can be completely shifted in the next war.

Combat uses dice, but it is a very interesting dice mechanic.  Roll 2D6, and substract the two numbers.  i.e. 5 & 2 is 3.  Doubles are BAD as they're always a 0 difference.  Also, roll that _adds_ up to 7 is an instant troop loss for you.

A bit of meta-game to learn with the chits, but the player aid made it clear.  I enjoyed the chits because their special abilities/alliances made your empire feel unique to your opponents due to limited quantities of each.  They provided a psuedo-civ building aspect to what is primarily a wargame.

RATING: 9
Superb area control game by Martin Wallace and an excellent simulation of 18th century warfare and grand strategy! The alliance mechanism is original and making the difference of two dice in combat prevents luck from playing a too high factor. The selection of a good set of tiles is crucial. The fixed number of turns ensures that the game will end in a reasonable timeframe, not like some other games where you have to be the last one standing and that drag on endlessly. ***** Try to control the German States and to secure a source of income via one of the colonies.

RATING: 9
I love this game! It has very simple rules but offers great number of strategies and ways to achieve victory which I'm amazed with. With very little you can do very much.

The alliance system is the second great feature. You are only able to define your allies and enemies at the beginning of the war, what makes negotiations and player out-of-the-game sympathies during the war less important.

The game has one little drawback. Because of great number of tiles and free choice between them, the game is overwhelming for beginners who simply don't know what to take.

RATING: 8
PLUS
Udany system sprzymierzeń (i jego licytacja) i jego wpływ na grę; dobra symulacja wydatków państwowych (na początku hulaj dusza, potem jest ciasno); masa różnych ulepszeń do wyboru, według którego prowadzi się politykę (uwzględnione aspekty militaryzmu, finansów, logistyki, dyplomacji); jest trochę stochastyki (walki, podróże). MINUS
Zdobywanie punktów ustalane przez banalny system większościowy.
SUMA
Partia symuluję (szczególnie w siódemkę) wyścig o ulepszenia dla własnego państwa, walkę o wpływy, ich obronę i żonglowanie kredytami i rewolucją wewnątrz własnego państwa.

PLUS
Good working alliance system (and the bidding for it) and its influence on the game; good simulation of state expenses (and of the change of attitudes: at the beginning "hulajdusza", towards the end "fin de siècle"); many various improvements to choose from for application in your state and shaping of your policy (aspects considered: military, finance, logistics, diplomacy); some stochastic implemented (battles, navigation).
MINUS
Points acquirement happens in a banal majority manner.
SUM
The game simulates (especially with seven players) the scramble for improvements for your country, the fight for influence and its protection and the juggling with credits and revolution inside your own country.

RATING: 7
Couple of games played. It takes some time to explain the rules, then ~3 hours to play. Normally I'm not a big fan of longer games, but SoE works. 

Some thoughts: there is always "situation going on" so SoE is full of interaction. Negotiation and diplomatic skills are needed, but at the end every problems can be solved using armies. Great. Byzantium and Perikles were a big disappointments for me, but Struggle of Empires is a good one. I'm glad, that Mr. Wallace can make a nice civilization games as well...

RATING: 7
Martin Wallace has done it again with another excellent design.  A fantastic influence/war game that seems to have similiar elements with (or a blend of) Adv Civ, El Grande and Princes of the Renaissance.  The Alliances system is innovative and very important to conflict, especially in the 3rd War phase.  A good deep game with lots of tile purchase combinations and tactical war elements and area scoring.  I'd personally rather play Princes of the Renaissance or Mare Nostrum (less analysis paralysis for me personally) a little more often, this is excellent design.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Excellent game with both strategic and tactical decisions to be made.  The game really seems to center around the alliance auction AND the unrest which comes in huge at the end of the game.  The tiles, while certainly not created equal, also really play out interestingly as there can be several powerful combos in the game.  And who knows, perhaps this game will mark the beginning of a mending of relationship between Tony and I as we were allied twice in the three wars...;-)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is brilliant - straightforward, yet deep with complex decisions. If you liked the world domination play of Risk, then this is the Euro version. Build your economy/style by purchasing tiles (abilities). Most of the tiles are very good, and their limited availability is a tense sub-game in itself. The world domination aspect doesn't have the sweeping advances like Risk does, (approve!), the combat is more sophisticated, and there is a naval component added. 

There are many tiles to coordinate, so SOE rewards repeated plays. Could have dropped the "Slavery" references with no loss in gameplay, (prefer "Tribal"). Too long with 7 players, 5 players is the sweet spot. 

Must play NOW!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Looks like a playable strategic scale game on the imperial Europe.  Always a welcome as it is a card multiplayer game.   The gameplay is abstract but the relevant and important elements are factored in.  The only thing that hit me is the lack of historicity, e.g. Russia may be selling Afican slaves to the North America.  All powers are the same with no special characters.  Game-wise, however, it offers quite an enjoyment to look for players interacting and expanding their empires.  This game would be more fun to play if you can find more players.

RATING: 3.5
[microbadge=7][microbadge=37554]

Things I didn't like about Struggle of Empires:
* Bidding for allies. For some reason this rubbed me wrong. It seemed like it just rewarded the people who were already doing well and had extra money to spend. The way it seemed, those who needed to attack someone because they were in a desperate situation may not be able to secure a position in the opposite alliance, thus negating their ability to enact a practical strategy.
* Getting control, and military not affecting scoring. Tiles that give you control aside (slavery, etc.), the way to get control is to beat someone in combat. This too seems to limit your options, making whole areas of the map potentially void of scoring opportunities, because there will be no control and no military there.
* The combat system. It's not complicated and I understand it, but it seems like it's going the long way about regardless for no important reason.
* What country you are. My favored country for games like this is England, just because I like her history. However, in this game, England is possibly the worst country to play. Austria and Prussia both have instant access to three European countries, and England has none; she isn't adjacent to anything. I understand the game actually tries to go far with its theme, but I think a bit of balance might have been a wiser idea this time.

RATING: 8
A very under-rated game where you are trying to gain control of colonies or Europe.  It is an area control game where you acquire control tokens by attacking local tribes or opposing players who control European powers.  It is a "war" game with very Euro mechanics.  You have all kinds of special powers that help you win wars and gain advantages.  

There are some print outs on the Geek that provide you with some chit numbers and tile organization which makes it easier for new players to learn where they need to concentrate.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The most disappointing game in some time.  I really wanted to like it based on theme and initial reading of the rules.  Instead it turned out to be a horrific blend of nearly perfect information, euro mechanics and a huge dose of luck.  The fun has been carefully sucked out of this game one mechanic at a time.  It was more of a struggle to finish the game than anything else.  The game is far too long for what it is.  The rules and components are far too fiddly, when it basically boils down to a very basic auction game and a dice game posing as an Uber-designer weurogame.  I'd rather play Modern Art and Roll Through the Ages a couple times.  Heck, I'd rather take out the trash, do the dishes and mop the floors than play this again.  It makes gaming a chore.  Bleah!

RATING: 3
Disclaimer: I love Martin (Brass is the first game I got signed in Essen), I love long and epic games, and I love dice. However, for this game, I just have to agree with everyone that gave this game a score of 3 or lower: Struggle of Empires combines the most unmatch elements in boardgames together: Long platime and high level of luck. Worse is that you need to spend at least 2 precious turns (total 15 in at least 3 hour game) to come back. Worse is that you get poorer because of just 1 unlucky rolls erly as the establishment by others would get them more money and better prepared for upcoming attacks. Worse is that the downtime between players is like hell.
If not for Martin, I would just give this game a 1. Sorry Martin, this game is just a piece of crap.

RATING: 4
[Played 4 times]
Several decisions, strong interaction between players, some diplomacy. Suffers downtime with more players, but the area majority and alliances aspects suffer with few players.
Occasional bad rolls can seriously damage a player (and between expert players, luck seems to play a greater role; e.g. "7" in battles). "Kingmaking" is possible and likely to happen. Tiles aren't balanced (on purpouse), but then their selection is bound to be repetitive with expert players. The best compromise between interaction, downtime, etc. is likely achieved with 5 players. Overall it's "too wargame rather than eurogame" and too long for what it offers (and the somewhat random outcome).

RATING: 6
Un juego de guerra con elementos de economía y control de zonas. REGALADO a César. Tres horas al menos de duración, con algunos tiempos muertos entre los turnos de cada jugador.
Primera partida de aprendizaje un poco lenta (2 guerras en 4 horas).
UPDATE: se rebaja de 8 a 7, porque el factor suerte está más presente de lo deseado: dónde se ponen los marcadores neutrales; los dados al overse en mar; los dados para la guera.
23 MAYO 2005: baja de 7 a 6. Nuevamente, pese a una relativmente buena estrategia que me llevó a ir ganando las dos primeras guerras, al final por un asutno de dados pasé a un tercer lugar.
Es decir, no es un juego en que uno se quede pensando en qué debí  hacer mejor (como In The Shadow of Emperors, Power Grid o Puerto Rico), sino que lamentando la mala suerte.
Entonces, un juego que dura 3 horas y que se decida por suerte, es mucho. Está bien para un filler o Ticket to ride, pero no para un juego que se presenta como serio.

RATING: 7.5
This is a very slick diplomatic game about European power politics in the age of discovery.  The engine is not very complicated, and the number of "exception" rules are manageable, but learning to play well is NOT a quick journey.  The depth of decision making (even without the diplomacy elements) is still over my head right now.  If only it did not take so long to play! To be fair, play speeds greatly up when you know what you are doing, but I don't believe the 3 hours stated time frame is going to be the norm.  I would say double that is not out of the question, especially on a first play through.

RATING: 7
11/02/05 Received. A lot of negative comments in this ratings section on this game are from wargamers who don’t like the Eurogame elements in SoE, and from Eurogamers who don’t like the wargame elements in the game.  Well, as a player of both types of games SoE is right up my alley.  I will say this, the multitude of tile types can overwhelm the uninitiated.  A good rules teacher is required to guide new players through which tiles are of the most value to them. And make sure you have the FAQ handy.  I wouldn’t recommend including players who start to fidget because they’re used to playing games that last at most 90 minutes. Expect about 3.5 hours. 
As of 10/04/15 I've played four times.

RATING: 9
Initial rating: Impressive! Although the rules seem quite easy, it's definitely not easy to play. Somebody wrote "complex, long & slow". All true, but for me not in a negative way.

It's quite complex, some of it thanks to the big amount of "alliance" & other special tiles. But it seems this game really fits together quite well & logically. It's definitely a long game, (longer than I usually want a game to last) but with no noticable downtime, since you're continously involved.

The first game went real slow, due to the complexity, trying to pick the best options, trying to adapt to what the other players do etc. But it all happens in a captivating way.

Played it with 6, first time for everyone, and everyone was very enthusiastic about this brilliant game and wants to play it again. Would have loved to just leave it on the table and play it again tomorrow...This is the first time I really wanted to give a 9 based on a first game.

Not something to be pulled off for a quick game, but definitely among the best choices if you're in for a gamers game that will last for an exciting evening.

My second game was a session with 7, with 2 newbies. It took 7 hours thanks to loads of A.P... But we had fun all the way.

Despite wanting to play this it took 3.5 years before I managed to play a third session, with 5 players, all experienced gamers. Much tougher.... Still a great game. Probably Wallace's best long game.
 
NL: erg goed strategisch lang spel. Regels lijken moeilijk, maar zitten best logisch in elkaar. Avondvullend spel. Nachtenvullend met 7.

RATING: 8
Wonderful, relatively simple empire, area majority game. Rules are, honestly, terrible, and there are some map ambiguities (at least to my mind) but there is a better rewrite on BGG under the game files. It's really more opaque than it should be. Gameplay is fairly complex, mechanics are simple. Definitely a lot to consider, especially when learning all the tiles. All in all, a typical Wallace stew of genius and some clunkiness.

But seriously -- this is a prime example of a horrible rules set.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pick actions, draft tiles and move your armies for area majority in the colonies.

Plays: A couple?
Type: Faux wargame = a game where you move military forces on a map, but where victory isn't intrinsically about conquest or destruction of enemies.
Complexity: Medium. For such a long game, the rules are surprisingly straightforward.
# of players: 5-7.
Playing time: 60 mins per player.
Player interaction: Conflict, Diplomacy and Competition for scarce resources - colonies and tiles.
Randomness: Medium - dice, with high impact but with control over when to roll and what odds to accept.

Replayability: Pretty high, there seems to be multiple paths to victory thanks to the tile drafting. Your empire "engine" will probably be different from game to game. The diplomacy also makes for an interactive game 
Fun: Good fun. The unique alliance mechanism is the most fun - you bid for your allies and you have to keep the alliance until the next bid. This creates wonderful tension as players try to outbid each other to end up in the stronger alliance. The resource management is also surprisingly fun here - balancing actions, population, money, armies and unrest is an interesting puzzle where you have to adapt to changing circumstances.
Memorability: Decent. Under the hood it's still a VP race. You are optimizing resources more than you are building an empire. Quick term gains are often more important than long term ones.

Thematic integration: Decent. Some mechanisms bring the colonialism theme home, like needing a ship off the coast of Africa to be able to take the Slavery action (no matter what you think of including slavery in a game). At heart it's a VP race though. The VP track running around the edge of the map is a sure sign.
Components: Functional. The map is suprisingly small for an epic game like this and it gets cluttered with counters at 7 players, but this is not much of a problem because the counters are cleanly printed with contrasting colours. It's not a pretty game, but it gets the job done.

Who will like this: People that like multi-player strategy games with conflict and don't mind slavery as a mechanism.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The choosing sides mechanic is inspired. How does Martin Wallace manage to evoke such theme and period with such essentially euro style?

RATING: N/A

RATING: 6
rating bases on one or two plays...i'm hoping to get this to the table again soon.  more info after that occurs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Multi-player area control game based on 18th Century European Colonial expansion. A clever bidding-based system establishes players into two alliances. But don't be fooled, this isn't in any way a cooperative game. The only thing that allies cannot do is attack each other - any other form of competition is OK. So, if you get into alliance with your biggest competitor he will find it difficult to take control of a region from you. There are lots of option tiles which allow you to tailor a strategy that suits you and you have to keep an eye on your population, treasury and unrest level as you forge your expansion plans. Like most Martin Wallace games everything is extremely tight - you always want to do much more than the number of actions allows. Very intense and involving game that will take at least 3 hours.

RATING: 8
I've really enjoyed both my solo run-through and one and only actual play of this with other players. I'm usually the one hating dice in games like this (see Perikles), but they didn't bother me at all here: it was extremely simple and was virtually deterministic when one opponent had any sort of clear advantage. There's some additional randomness in the chit pulls that seed the map prior to each war, but everyone sees this ahead of time and [i]then[/i] gets to bid on turn order. Compare this to something like Eclipse, where all the random "exploration" is individual: I flip a tile, it sucks, and only I am screwed. Anyway, after muddling through the rules and the first war, we accelerated rapidly; I'd be surprised if this really took three hours with five players. The Alliance mechanism is excellent and can win or lose someone the game; playing that right is key to the game. Very interested in playing this again.

All in all a solid game with a very good thematic touch and certainly not a game I would call overly complex or heavy.

RATING: 7
This game is excellent in my book, though I prefer the Conquest of the Empire version to this.  Downgraded from a 10 after further play as the political element can be quite brutal so you have to have very thick skin.  If I'm in the right mood with the right group I'm ok.  With the wrong group it can really suck, actually, esp. if someone starts kingmaking or doing things just for "shits and giggles".The abundant number of choices for actions (and limited number of actions) in the game between tiles/regions/economy and gaining victory points is quite a mental "struggle". The mechanism of maintaining a balance between military ventures and area control and keeping unrest to a minimum is very well designed.  Then you have to balance population and income on top of that!! The addition of bidding for turn order and alliances is totally unique and changes this into much more than a war game as money is very tight and must be managed carefully with colonial ventures.  Beautiful map and tokens and tiles.  First Class and Highly recomommended. Rule Book gives very helpful tips on game play which is a feature many games leave out.

RATING: 6
In 18th century warfare, buy tiles, move armies, attack or populate/enslave nations in this Martin Wallace area control game. Novel mechanic is bidding for alliances (you cannot attack allies) at the beginning of each epcoh. Combat resolved by rolling 2 dice, and taking the difference of the dice as your combat strength.  Many things going on with unrest counters, tiles, actions of your opponents, etc. Dicing off in combat, and the usual problems with area control wargames on picking on the leader(s) still leads to a not so good taste in the mouth. The games are long too! Expect 4 hrs for 6 players, and probably five hours with 7 players. Sorry Martin Wallace, I really wanted to like this game, but the dice off in combat makes this game too variable. I suspect this game plays best with 4 players - even number of players so no unbalanced alliances and turns go quicker.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The alliance system is brilliant.

RATING: 8
Very good game, but not perfect. The name is very well chosen as playing it feels like a struggle between the Empires. The rulebook is terrible and the map design could have been so much better. Why waste so much space on UK, France, Spain while you don't need that map piece? Why place water on the border of Germany while you cannot go there with a ship? The alliance piece is the brilliant element in the game, but the random 30 countries markers that appear during the game influence too much what direction the game will take. Also, it is impossible, even with an alliance, to come back and win the game when you had some losses or bad luck with the dice. And then it takes a long time to finish. There are some very good elements for sure and it is a great game to play. But it is inferior in terms of some of the alliance and negotiation game elements that you can find in a game like Diplomacy (which obviously takes even longer to play, but has zero luck and has a flawless design).

RATING: 9
My initial impressions of this game are very positive. It is reminiscent of Empires of the Ancient World, with its simple map, confusing rules, three turns each divided into several rounds, a generic rather than specific historical situation, and a blending of Euro influence game and multi-player wargame mechanics. I think Struggle of Empires is a significant step forward from the earlier game. In particular, the alliance auction is a very clever mechanic which prevents the game from being a typical multiplayer beat-up-the-leader negotiation game.

RATING: 8.5
-

I like this game a lot, although I in my first game I had my frustrating moments when the dice were especially unfriendly. I ended up with a tie for the most "unrest" tokens, which subtract from your total victory points. You get an unrest token every time you lose a battle, which are settled by dice rolling. Even though the dice have a big role in determining battle results, the effect of dice is not blatant and pre-planning for defense and attack and choosing your battles wisely would minimize the luck element. 

The rules are long and you need at least a couple of hours to go over them the first time, and there are lots of various tiles and counters, but the gameplay becomes much more clear after a few rounds and you start to enjoy it. Once players know the rules and are familiar with the various tiles, I don't think a full game will take longer than 3.5 hours.  Good balance for a game in which everyone has the option to attack one or two opponents on their turn. The Alliance feature is a nice mechanic of the game and enhances the long term strategy. This game is worth the time investment.
[Based on 4 plays. First played 03/19/05.]

RATING: 7.25
A great civilization variant with a handful of key mechanics which makes this stand out from the average bulk of civ games. The bidding for alliances bid is an amazing mechanic and really what this whole game is about. Of course, apart from the official alliances, there is no limited as to how many spoken agreement can be made and broken, lots of backstabbing and negotiation going on. I have played it only once but found that some players overreact whenever you become to vicious, despite the fact that the game and theme calls for being ruthless, so be careful who you play with, this is a sure-fire temper riser. The initial setup adds some randomness which makes this game way more re-playable than other civ games. It takes a while to learn all the available upgrades and such by heart but once you manage to, the game is actually very simple. The board and card artwork could do with an overhaul, both in style as well as usability but that is a minor issue. All in all, an above than average civ game which should be your holy grail if you are really into the subject matter. It is not my preferred theme and as I said, I am usually a nasty player and like to be vicious and cruel when playing games which call for it and found that many geeks cannot take it because to them, gaming is everything and they cannot lose. Great game. HX

RATING: 10

RATING: 6.5
The more I play this game (which is not very often considering its length), the less I like it.

Cons:
* This game plods. The only way to get points is to get a majority of control tokens. There is really only one way to get these tokens. Even though there are mechanisms that enable you to secure these tokens more easily (in the form of purchasing special powers), there is no way of getting them faster. Add this to the limited number of actions you can do during your turn, and the fixed number of turns during the game, SoE feels very procedural; there isn't any drama -- there are no "big" moves or dramatic changes of leaders.
     Unfortunately, the only souce of drama is the area-majority scoring mechanic, which is so overused, but for a reason; it effectively creates dramatic swings in scoring.
* Thematically very abstracted and dry. The area-majority scoring as well as the auctioning for alliances don't mesh well with the colonization theme.
* The alliance auction mechanism limits free-for-all attacking, which perhaps stabilizes the game, but because these auctions only happen (I think) three times per game, it feels like it actually limits your options, making the game far less dynamic.

Pros:
* I really like the economic layer in the game in which you are free to choose any special power available. In addition, all powers are affordable at the start of the game so players can pursue different strategies.

Symmary: 
Wallace truly designs Euro-wargame hybrids. The wargame part is always welcome; it introduces direct conflict and dice rolling, but the Euro part -- constraining player actions and conflicts between players and unthematic game mechanisms -- are becoming very tiresome for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A good game in the Risk super-family, where you balance various currencies (money population unrest) with your military needs.  It gains marks for trying to regulate player diplomacy with game mechanics.  These mechanics half work, as by joining the same Alliance as your rival you protect yourself from their overt aggression, however you can still be hurt by malicious tile play.  Where they fall down is that non-aggression pacts can as easily go across Alliances as within them, and Alliance partners are not compulsorarily drawn into each other's wars.
Pluses are that the game is fairly clean and fast-playing (for what it is - we're talking 3-4 hours), and not too chaotic given the number of players.  Negatives are that the open availability of all tiles makes the first few turns very important but tricky for new players, that leader bashing is still the main factor, there are a few mis-judged chromey/lucky rules such as sinking on sea voyages, the system is a bit too grainy so losing a battle involving a couple of units is a major disaster.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Strong Martin Wallace multiplayer design which definitely improves with replays. The tempo and value of tile draws in the game changes with the number of players and strategies shift with shifting groupthink. The changing alliances in each turn and bidding structure make the game very interesting indeed.

RATING: 7
An area control/influence game played over three rounds (wars), each including auctioning for alliances, troop introduction and movement, tile acquisition for development, and dice-based conflict to gain/replace influence. Auctioning to newly define alliances at the start of each round allows players to use gold to build or break support and is unique, making gold more important than you think. Movement rules are somewhat confusing, and should have been simplified. The dice mechanic for battle attempts to be different (calculation of the *difference* between two rolls) but may as well have been simplified, as it adds nothing new from a strategy and tactics standpoint. Components are fiddly and not as good as the wooden bits introduced in the Treefrog line; although the rules are easy to learn and very well written. Lots of luck introduced by the dice -- I prefer more strategy. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Like most of Wallace's games this is best played with experienced players, since the alliance phase is the crucial aspect of the game.

Not sure if an odd number of players would make a difference.

With new players it would seem common for some players to get behind and stay behind. but with experienced players it would seem that the alliances would be conducive to catching up.

The basic strategy would seem to have a power region and have the company tile for that region, then try to get a presence in as many of the other regions as possible. 

First in a region is great but difficult to hold without a lot of effort. Several second places in regions, especially Europe is essential to staying competitive.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Great game - very much a Euro-wargame, it feels quite a bit like El Grande with combat, where players are competing for 1st/2nd/3rd place area majorities.  The bidding for who you're allied with and who you're opposing each of 3 wars is the most novel aspect of the game and quite an elegant way to simulate unstable, changing alliance, compared to something like the more complex alliance tracks in Napoleonic Wars.  Downtime can be a bit high with 7 players, especially if your plans get blocked and you don't have much to do effectively towards the end of each War.  There's no attempt to be historical really, which means it doesn't really matter which country you play to pursue the strategy you want. I do prefer the semi-historical nature of Imperial where each county does tend to have a common strategy from game to game even if that doesn't really match anything historical.  Which tiles you take really dictates what type of strategy you're going to pursue that game, in addition to the starting locations of your 5 control markers. The "map" is very abstracted in terms of movement, which can be a bit difficult to grasp (e.g. you can't move armies by land to adjacent areas in the Americas - you just need to have a ship in the area you're moving to).  And combat can be surprisingly swingy, even with the odd nature of the dice rolling (adding the difference between 2d6 to your army strength).  But all that said, it's an amazing game for someone looking for a large-player count Euro with combat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Mmmmmmm, Martin Wallace goodness....! This is a game that I try and play every chance I get, but never seem to be able to get it on the table. Much more an Area Influence game that a Wargame, I feel that this is almost the very definition of a Waro. You only get two actions per turn, and they can be whatever you want. Although there is about 10 things you want to do, and if you don't do at least 4 of them, your screwed!  I love the "cards" in this game, that are actually rectangular counters, and the artwork and bit quality are beautiful. Units are all generic, and only the countries with influence counters on them (drawn randomly at the beginning of each round (of which there are three)) can be targeted for VP's. Influence counters can also only be replaced, never added, except through the random drawings. So once a player takes it over, it must be converted by another player, by attacking! The person with the most Influence markers in a country at the end of a round recives that countries max VP's. There are lower VP amounts for second place, and sometimes even some for third. Attacking is pretty easy, and fairly common, considering it has to take place in order for players to win. The action "cards" give some advantages, and helps with certain situations. There is an economic factor also, which is called Unrest, and you get that if you can't pay for your armies, or other things, and the person with the most Unrest immediatly loses the game, at final scoring. There is also the famed alliance mechanic. At the beggining of each round, there is an auction for who's on who's "team". If you have an alliance with somebody, then you can't attack them. This brings the value of money up very high. Rich players can guarantee that thier closest rival can't attack them, and vice versa. It can be very interesting, with many a back stabbed. I really like this game for its Area Influence aspect, abstract movement, and the alliance factor.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
"constant warfare" (as the period is referred to in the Historical settings) is a good description of the game too. I felt a little disappointed that the game is essentially an all out wargame. And I mean all out. The rules are muddled and need a serious dose of editing to give them a structure. Additionally the rules are vague and ambiguous - as is the board. Very disappointing that yet again certain things in a Warfrog game didn't seem very well thought out - such as the third victory point level in the Ottoman Empire that can never be achieved. Once again Wallace has come so close and unfortunately overshot the mark.Simplifying some of the rules might have been a good start - my poor little brain is never going to remember all the ifs and buts in the rules between games. (Perhaps the designer could have got rid of square symbols on tiles and just made them the same as the dark grey keep and use once only tiles. Maybe put rope between North America, the Caribbean and South America - Spain to Morocco too. Copper coins = one gold, while silver coins = five gold? Huh? Who thought that up? What about following convention and make small gold = one gold and large gold = five gold?  Don't write sentences in the rules like "Naval combat and land combat follow the same rules- Each army/navy has a value of one. Forts have a value of 2 when defending..." You know it's not unheard of in real life for some forts to defend against attack from the sea - I certainly misinterpreted this rule until I read the contradiction by the designer, and etc.)One feature of the game I like is the combat dice roll system. The probability of a high number outcome versus a low number is very clever, and  extra losses on a roll of seven is brilliant. Another redeeming factor is that it's a good multiplayer wargame if played regularly. Finally, it just seemed to be the wrong game for my group - it was a bit too rule heavy for them.

RATING: 10
After the first play, I thought about this on the drive home and the next day. This was by far the least "fiddly" Wallace game I've played. All of the mechanics seemed straightforward and made sense in the context. I love all of the different tiles--it allows for various strategies to try out, and you can combine some into interesting and cool "combos." I don't think the luck is out of whack--though good heavens, one guy had rotten luck with the dice. I lost 3 ships myself to bad dice...and if I pursue a "colonial strategy" again, I'd probably pick Navigation early. 

The auction for alliances and turn order is a great mechanic. It's a tense and vital part of the game--underestimate it at your peril. 
A winner for me. I'd definitely play this again, and it proves that Martin Wallace can produce a nonfiddly game that just works.

Update: Played again, and it might be a 10. What a great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Dry amalgam of war- and eurogame. Has neither fixed nor free alliances, which embezzles player interaction by neglecting both team-play and backstabbing.

There are only two ways to gain influence in countries and therefore victory points: 

1) Combat. Bad idea if you and your opponent are the only ones who battle it out, because this'll costs you the game. 

2) Luck. Initial set-up and random events that happen to occur when it's your turn.

There are some clever design ideas, but the game suffers from all drawbacks a victory point track bestows upon a multiplayer-game. It also drags on too long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Sok jó ötlet, amit elront egy kicsit az a fránya kockadobálás. Röviden így tudnám összefoglalni a SoE-t. Minden játékos egy nagyhatalmat irányít, akik 3-szor 6 kört játszanak 4 játékos esetén. 6 kör után van pontozás a jelenlegi elfoglalt területek szerint. 11 területért megy a harc, ami mint az El Grandéban pontot ad, az ott elhelyezkedő befolyásszámunk után. Minden helyen a második helyezett is kap pontot, néhány helyen a harmadik is. A játékban hajó és szárazföldi egységek vannak, a harc pedig kockadobással dől el. Jó ötlet, hogy a hajók csak támogatják a szárazföldi erőket, az ő csatájuk csak a támogatás eldöntéséről szól. Jó ötlet, hogy legrosszabb kockadobás esetén is maximum két egysége veszhet a vesztesnek. Két kockával dob mindkét fél és a két kocka különbségéhez adja hozzá a csatában szereplő egységeinek számát, illetve egyéb bónuszait. A nagyobb értékkel rendelkező győz és cserélhet egy ott levő befolyásjelzőt a sajátjára az ellenfélétől. Az ellenfél egy egységet veszít, bármelyik fél hetes dobása esetén az adott fél még egyet. A játék ott kompenzál a rossz dobásért, hogy a kör végén minden egység pénzbe kerül, kevesebb egységgel több bevételünk lesz. A játékot a különböző lapkák dobják föl, amik különböző bónuszokat nyújthatnak a harcban vagy pénzbevételben. A pénz a csaták indításához kell, illetve lapkák szerzéséhez. Új egység felállítása popuplációpontba kerül, amiből mindenki 3-szor kap 5 darabot. Ez is egy jó megoldás. A legérdekesebb része a játéknak a diplomácia eldöntése. A megfelelő játékos két játékost beállíthat, akik egymással szemben állnak az adott nagykörben, ezt több pénz beadásával teljesen megváltoztathatja más és ez így tulajdonképpen licitálással dől el. Ez dönti el az adott nagykör játékossorrendjét is. Tehát nagyon más lehet ki kivel van egy-egy nagykörben. Ez nagyon ötletes rész. A kockadobás ellenére teljesen kiegyenlített volt a játék. Szóval nagyon vegyes az összkép. Érezhetően jó játékról van szó, de az ilyen kockásoknál mindig benne van az, hogy ha valaki rosszul dob sokszor, akkor nincs esélye sajnos. Látszik, hogy komolyan van tesztelve és mivel az élmény is jó volt így 7-est adok rá, főleg mert négyen is tökéletesen működött, többen pedig még jobb lehet véleményem szerint.

RATING: 9
While this game is certainly not trying to be a historical simulation in any specific sense, I am still very impressed with the way it does distill and convey the major strategic issues faced with 18th century powers, culminating with the threat of the French revolution in background (represented by unrest). Seen purely as a game, this is certainly one of Wallace's most inspired systems.

As a downside, I am disturbed by the generic feel each power has, and have hence proposed minimalist adjustments to set-up which might affect balance and re-playability but certainly also help giving the game some historical plausibility (see Variants section on the game's page). to my taste, that greatly improves the overall experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
What a great game.  The Alliance/Turn order decision is brilliant.  Too many tough choices for what little action that you have makes this gripping right up to the end. I'm so glad that they decided not to call it Birth of Nations.  

It really feels like you are building your Empire from the ground up.  The theme is emersive and works well with all the different upgrades or bonuses you can get.  Keeping the balance between keeping up with the tile race and grabing position on the board is vital.  I love the way that Unrests works.  The game just amps up as you progress.  

This game gets a 9 with an even number of people and a 10 with an odd number of players.  I now only pursue this with 5 or 7.  Crazy things happen when you stack odd alliances.  

Tons of great choices with too little ability to do them from beginning till the end, Wallace shinning as usual.

RATING: 8
Based on two three player games.  I think it will move it if playing with 5 or more, but I need to get a game in to be sure.  With three, the alliance system and diplomacy aspects don't have a very large impact.

I love the unlimited funds for unrest transfer and the tile system.  El Grande is one of my 2-3 favorite games and if the multiplayer really works here this could be El Grande plus!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.75
A very "meh" game to me. The mechanisms are extremely clunky and are very typical of a Martin Wallace design. There are weird rules for movement to colonies, with rolling a 1 on a d6 and then a 1-2 kills, 3-4 sends back, and 5-6 completes. Extremely complex and arbitrary for no real reason. Also, the combat has both sides rolling 1d6 - 1d6 and adding it to their attack value, which gives a very large variance in combat results.

Additionally, there is a large amount of randomness in what influence tiles are drawn, and an even more pigeonholing effect through your starting influence locations.

Also, the improvement tiles are grossly overpowered. The extra free units are extremely clutch in comparison to some of the others.

Besides all that (which is a lot of faults) there are some interesting mechanisms involved. I did enjoy the alliance system, that really made you think about positioning and placement. But for the type of game it is, I'd rather just play a better area control type game.

RATING: 8
This is a difficult game to rate after one play. The game is an area majority, conflict game over three phases. The in game decisions around special abilities, unit creation, movement and economics are difficult and seem to have a range of subtlety and nuance. This is pure Martin Wallace, 'black cubes', two actions per turn stuff, slightly dry in presentation whilst still being thematic in play and with enough weird rules to keep you coming back to the rulebook. This is the type of game I love but it is long and the production quality could be higher but those are minor issues. 

My big concern is that these decisions might be overshadowed by the alliance phase where players bid to place each other into one of two temporary alliances, the outcomes of which can make or break any strategy. It is also possible to be knocked out of the running and still have to play for another hour or two. As games for up to 7 players go this is awesome, and it didn't hurt that I scraped a 1 point victory.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played this game for the first time this past weekend.  I am already hooked and cannot wait to play this one again! I think after a few more plays, it could be a 10.  Easy game to explain.  Not a ton of time is spent having to teach it to others.  It is a fun, light wargame!  I see the complaints about luck being involved, and yes, there is some luck in it, but how many games don't have some degree of luck?  If people complain about luck, then they'll be complaining about all games for the rest of their life.  Granted, some games have more luck and dice involved than others, but I found this one to have a great balance of both.  Everyone should play this one at least once.....but after that first play, I can guarantee you'll want to play it again!

RATING: 10
Simple yet deep, with high player interaction, and lots of choices each turn without being overly complex.  This is my favorite game.  Requires some House Rules to make a two-player game work best, but it is possible to play with that player count.  It takes some time to learn about what all the different tiles do; however, the rule book is small and straightforward.  It is by no means a historically simulation (Germany colonizing South America or India?), but having the freedom to explore all the possibilities is fun.

RATING: 9
Just learning the rules now and waiting for the right opportunity to bring this one out. I'm excited to give it a go.

1/11/13 - After one play, this is in my top 10. In fact, it's #2 currently. There is a solid economic system driving the conflict throughout the entire game, which I love! Especially when the economy has been created by Wallace. Perhaps that's why I love it so much, because while I don't usually gravitate to a dudes-on-a-map type game. But this one stands alone as one with many more interesting decisions, that go beyond the typical war-game questions (like "Who do I attack this turn?"). 

If you can get a solid bunch of players together, that have enough time to dedicate to a longer, but nail-biting experience, and you want a war game that feels like an economic war as much as a blood war, I don't think there's a better option than Struggle of Empires.

RATING: 6.5
I give it 6.5. It doesn't excite me but usually if I give something a 6.0 it is tending towards inherently flawed.

I was surprised, not in a bad way, by how abstract this is. The movement restrictions are just enough to make it seem like it's really a world map. I also really like how there is a limited amount of control to fight over. If you want presence in a region, it's at somebody else's expense. You don't create colonies out of thin air just by "placing influence cubes" or something.

The alliance system is nice, making it harder to directly smack whoever you want to fight. Though it does seem a little weird to use cash to bid on it. Also, whenever the system [i]doesn't[/i] restrict you, I sometimes feel disturbingly little guidance from the game regarding who I should go attack.

I wish the game were simpler. You have to take unrest tokens to borrow money, but you can only borrow what you need. Makes almost all transactions very tedious. I think there are a few too many different tiles also. Quite daunting at first.

As an area control game, what seems missing is some natural way for the controller of an area to lose control. One is likely to become dominant in a region or two, and hard to oust. It's worth more points to have smaller presence in a larger number of regions, but this is both harder to defend, and makes you a leader that people want to bash.

The game is made up of three "wars," and it takes most of a war to pull off a task like "go here and knock the guy who owns it out of there." I just don't get the feeling that much happens or that it will be very surprising.

...I wish I had taken more notes on what happened. Despite everyone knowing who needed to be bashed in the third war, that leader still won the game easily. It's alarming to wonder if the game was already decided. But it could be that I just don't understand the game well enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
[Rated 2005/05/07 based on 1 play]

Learnt to play this back-to-back with learning to play Power Grid, which was not the best plan - my brain was left dribbling from my ears after 6-7 hours between the two.

Long, a bit fiddly, but very much worth the effort.  There is so much to think about, and never anywhere near enough resource to do all the things you want to do - everything you accomplish feels like an achievement.

The ever-changing alliances mean screwage is kept reasonable, as you almost certainly will be back teamed with the person you backstabbed later in the game.  It prevents a runaway leader from beating up on *everyone* at once too.

I can't ever seeing myself getting the time it deserves for repeated plays to really learn it though.

RATING: 7
A dizzying array of choices that vex even seasoned gamers.  More paths to victory that Catan has sheep.  But even the best player can be foiled by unlucky dice rolls.  In my first-ever game, two of the heavyweights at the table were compeletey robbed by a series of unlucky rolls.  I ended up tying for the victory.  Interesting, but I can think of more fun & exciting ways to spend 6 hours at the game table.
I've been thinking about this today, and think my initial reaction was too harsh.  The bidding-for-allies-&-turn-order mechanic in the game is excellent: for two wars we didn't care very much what happened, in the third, the bidding was furious, with players pooling money to get their desired result.  The luck factor was manageable to a certain degree (though truly bad rolls, like x y=7 or 1 1, REALLY hurt).  Whittling down your possible actions to two per turn was interesting, as is the civil unrest factor, which helped me tie for the win.  Finally, the shifting alliances caused people who had helped each other in previous wars to go toe-to-toe just minutes later.

RATING: 8.5
I finally got to "play" this one, well at least through one war.  We are typically a slow group, so I knew it would be hard to fit in the time allowed.  Once we got going, I was surprised at how fast it was moving.  The problem was that it really wasn't going all that fast, it only seemed that way.

I was surprised at how tight the actions were during the first war.  I thought the number of options would be overwhelming, and maybe it was at first, but once the easy country tiles started to dry up, so did the available actions. It was an alliance of three against two, and I was on the two side.  The two of use became the only source of additional control markers, and there was no way to defend everything.  Once they had picked off the easy targets, we could afford to make our remaining holdings a more expensive target.  The war ended just as the situation was becoming really tight, and while I have only played this one partial game, I bet that tension is no accident.

I look forward to seeing how the character of the game changes in wars 2 and 3.  We could have continued the game later, but we decided to start our next game from scratch to benefit from our new experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Gets better with every play.  Masterful flourishes take a vanilla area-majority game and turn it into a wonderfully nuanced and thematic piece.  The alliance system is mind-bending, the money always tight, and the unrest a work of genius.  The interleaving between the tiles is both easily grasped and subtle.  There's even space for negotiation and bribery.  This is the best Wallace game I've played, and I like a lot of his efforts.  It's crisper and less wonky that most of his war-themed games, and it's less dry than his economic affairs.  There's fewer gotcha rules in this game than the others, and the whole comes together very clearly.  The ability to share ties for area majority also means that there is a symbiosis possible that is very unique.  Make sure you play it with 5+ players, preferably 6 or 7 to get the most out of the alliance system.

RATING: 8
The alliance mechanic in SoE is the most ingenious solution I have ever seen to the bash-the-leader problem in multi-player conflict games. My friend calls this "Risk-done-right" and I agree- this is my favorite dudes on a map game by far. The "you count the difference between the two dice" system elegantly condenses the probability distribution to make results feel less arbitrary while still delivering the hope and drama of unexpected outcomes on occasion. Make no mistake Princes of the Renaissance is the deeper and more interesting game, but SoE is just plain fun. The myriad of special tiles add that chrome which really enhances the flavor of the game. Best with 5 or 7!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It has a good turn angst, but it also has a lot of down time, with not much that you can do until it's your turn, since so much can happen between them.  I'd like to play it some more.

RATING: 8
After owning this game since it was released, I dared to play this game at last on 18 DEC 2005... Not that it's a difficult game, but this was for me the first time that the Warfrog rules were hard to grasp. On reading it makes all sense, but a solo-play gave me many questions and I kept rereading the rules. I own Age of Steam, Liberté, Prinses of the Renaissance and Empires of the Ancient World, but the rules of these games gave me less problems. So I waited until the game was fully covered here on BGG before I picked this game up again.

The player aids here on BGG, especially Innovan's work (compliments on his FAQ/Quick Reference/Sheets for Tiles and Country counters), was just the right stuff I needed to teach and play SoE.

My first game was a 2 player learning game with a friend of mine. The few questions I didn't know directly from the rules and the FAQ were all covered in the FAQ. Then I played the game on my regular gaming club. A four player game, 3 newbies...

Two of them had checked the game out here on BGG on my advice. The third one was completely blank on the game. Board setup/ Rules explenation took about 45 minutes. We started playing, it went smoothly. All my preparations on this game really helped.

It took us 3 hours to finish the game. Time flew by, it's a tense game with so much to want and only 2 actions on a turn. I ended dead last with -7 points for first place on unrest. I had 55 points, number 3-60 points, number 2-62 and number 1-64 points.

With the right tiles you can conquer the dice. Maybe one/two times the dice were against a player.

People who like games like Vinci and/or Wallenstein, should try and play this one. One warning; this game takes a (maybe too) steap learning curve if no one has prepared this game well. I personally think people will not enjoy this game without someone who has really prepared the first session; because people will start asking questions and you better know most answers...otherwise you will be playing a 'rules reading game'.

Also; Just reading the rules and playing the game is asking for trouble in my opinion. The FAQ is essential!

You can find it at Innovans site, look at the link section for SoE. After reading and rereading this file and using his player aids the game fell in place for me.

Recommended gamers-game! I've put it from my HOT 10 list to my PERSONAL TOP 10.

RATING: 7
Enjoyable enough. I wasn't wowed by it but then again I did play one 6.5 hour game and didn't get bored. If you like keeping multiple threads in your head and working for your own ends while also thwarting those of others it can be a lot of fun. I like the general idea and strategies involved as well as the overall thematic feel of warring empires. I didn't feel compelled to run out and buy it right away, but I would definitely play again.

RATING: 7
Good game. The alliances are shifting so fast that they mean virtually nothing. It is short-term Realpolitik that matters. The only thing I personally lack in the game is the symmetry between players. I would have preferred each power to have a unique trait, if nothing else in order to make players change strategies when taking charge of a new country. You will learn little about the time period, but it is a eurogame after all.

RATING: 4
Pros: neat historic sweep, the tile powers

Cons: TOO LONG. Not enough happens. Debt-rule problem. Alliance mechanic deepens runaway effect. Tiles might not be priced correctly (reliant on order bidding to sort of correct), and a handful are very powerful. Combat results a bit too variable. You should probably should cap your die diff result to 3 (and perhaps just roll 1d6/2 round down?), and maybe tone down the extra casualty on '7's to some weaker penalty.

Likely needed more development. Might be excusable if the game were 2 hours long instead of 7.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
4/24/09 -- After 1.5 plays, I'm rating this a 6.  A fairly solid game, especially considering it's predecessor, the disastrous Princes of the Renaissance.  In general, longer multiplayer games hold a lot of appeal for me, but I tend to not like big leader-bashing/kingmaking end-game scenarios, which this game has bigtime.  In Struggle of Empires, you try to stay conspicuously in 2nd or 3rd place right up until the end, and then try to time a power grab for first at just the right time after having tried to convince everyone else to take down the leader.  For me, this just isn't very interesting, and I'd rather play something where the designer has at least made an effort to address this (rather common) issue.  Dune, Cosmic Encounter, Civilization and Titan, for example (all big, multi-player games), all provide differing and effective mechanisms for avoiding this problem.
